This is the most far-reaching consequence of contagious sin — not just individual conscience suppression (CHAR-4) or individual refusal to repent (CHAR-5) but the collective redirecting of a whole community's conscientious moral life. 2Ki 21:11 — "made Judah also to sin" — the community's conscientiousness now produces sinning as its collective output, shaped by the leader's transmitted direction.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that political revolt can operate with fully engaged volition and action while the moral-awareness component is suppressed or absent in the revolting party. This is consistent with the broader pattern: the narrator supplies the moral weight (pa.sha as transgression) that the revolting parties themselves do not appear to register in the corpus. The characteristic thus reveals a *selective conscientiousness* — the political-strategic components operative, the moral component not evidenced.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that sinful speech is not laziness or inattention — it is *active, conscientious, morally-engaged speech in the wrong direction*. 1Ti 6:4 — the person "has an unhealthy craving for controversy and quarrels about words" — they are investing real conscientiousness in the pursuit of controversial sinful speech. This explains why sinful speech is so difficult to correct: it is not an accidental failure of moral engagement but a thorough moral engagement directed toward the wrong end.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that CHAR-10's mechanisms are *conscientious sinning pathways*: the person who is enticed, acts hypocritically, or commits apostasy is not morally passive or negligent but is engaging the conscientiousness system — awareness, volition, and action — in the direction of the mechanism. 2Pe 2:14's trained enticers are the paradigm: fully conscientious, morally aware, volitionally committed, and actively engaged — in the service of sinful enticing. This makes the characteristic's mechanisms particularly effective: they recruit the conscientiousness system rather than bypassing it.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that sin as universal condition produces the *most severe form of conscientiousness fracture*: not the absence of moral awareness (the mind knows God's law — Rom 7:25) or the absence of moral will (the "I" does not will the sinful acts — Rom 7:17/20) but the severance of the knowing-willing-acting chain at the acting level. This fracture is the deepest diagnostic evidence of the condition's constitutional depth: it operates not by eliminating conscience or will but by capturing the body's members as instruments that override both, producing action that the conscience condemns and the will did not choose. This is the unique contribution of CHAR-11 to the M10 characterological analysis: sin-as-condition produces a divided self that knows and wills the good while doing the evil.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that sin's enslaving power is built by conscientious practice: the person is not careless but actively and repeatedly sinning (1Jo 3:8 — "makes a practice"). This explains why the enslaving power is so deeply entrenched: it is not the product of accidental or inattentive acts but of deliberate conscientious practice that has progressively formed the will into the slave-condition (Joh 8:34 — from practice to slavery). Breaking the enslaving power requires not merely awareness or willpower but a constitutional transformation that only new birth can provide (1Jo 3:9).

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that the divine record is both the product of conscientiousness misdirected (sin deliberately accumulated — Isa 5:18; 30:1) and the stimulus for conscientiousness rightly directed (the made-known sin → confession → restitution → offering cycle — Lev 4:23; Num 5:7). The record thus stands at the hinge of conscientiousness: it is what conscientiousness produced when misdirected, and it is what conscientiousness must address when correctly engaged.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that the seeking dimension requires full conscientiousness (moral awareness of sin + volitional commitment to seek + action of seeking — Exo 32:30; Act 2:38; 2Ch 29:23/24), while the receiving dimension restores the person to a more fully functioning conscientiousness (Col 2:13 — alive; Act 2:38 — Spirit received). The characteristic is thus a conscientiousness event: conscientious seeking produces a restored conscientiousness through forgiveness received.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that the generational sinning-pattern is not a failure of conscientiousness but its misdirection: Psa 78:17 — "rebelling against the Most High" — the rebellion is conscientiously enacted (deliberately, persistently). This makes the condition more entrenched, not less: a community that is conscientiously committed to a wrong direction is harder to turn than one that has merely drifted. The heart-turning (1Ki 8:47) requires redirecting a genuine conscientiousness rather than awakening a dormant one.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The conscientiousness pattern confirms the T3.7 agency finding: the sinner as moral character is active rather than passive. The sinner-identity does not produce moral torpor but conscientious engagement in the wrong direction. This makes the identity more entrenched than mere passivity would: the conscientious sinner has invested their full moral capacity in the sinner-direction. Transformation requires not awakening dormant conscientiousness but redirecting active conscientiousness — which is why the repentance (Luk 18:13; Luk 15:7) requires the full engagement of turning, not merely stirring.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that guilt-as-inner-state, when operating through engaged conscience, produces the highest form of conscientious moral action in M10: the Lev 4/5/6 and Num 5:7 restitution sequences are examples of conscientious precision (full restitution + added fifth) directed entirely toward the wronged party's restoration. This is the characteristic's functional value: guilt-recognition redirects conscientiousness from the self-serving direction that created the guilt toward the other-repairing direction that resolves it.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The conscientiousness pattern confirms CHAR-18's active character: iniquity-as-accumulated-crime is not produced by passive moral drift alone but by conscientiously-directed wrongdoing (Isa 5:18 — cart ropes) and heart-level allegiance choices (Eze 14:3). The accumulated iniquity thus reflects the full investment of the person's conscientiousness faculty in the wrong direction. This is why its reversal requires the equally conscientious acts of confession (Lev 26:40), heart-humiliation (Lev 26:41), and word-taking (Hos 14:2) — conscientiousness must be redirected, not merely awakened.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The conscientiousness pattern confirms CHAR-19's active character: the deliberate boundary-crosser is not passively drifting but conscientiously pursuing the transgression (Amos 4:4) or its concealment (Pro 28:13). This conscientiousness applied to transgression is what makes CHAR-19 the most morally serious category in M10: not negligence but purposeful wrongdoing. The directional reversal of conscientiousness (from transgression-commission to transgression-forsaking) is the measurable inner sign of genuine repentance (Pro 28:13 — "confesses and forsakes").

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The conscientiousness pattern confirms that faithlessness is not passive drift but active deployment of the person's moral capacity in the wrong direction. Isa 24:16's triple intensification — "the traitors have betrayed, with betrayal the traitors have betrayed" — names the repeated, conscientious investment in betrayal. This active-conscientiousness dimension distinguishes CHAR-20 from passive weakness: the faithless person does not accidentally betray but conscientiously enacts and conceals the betrayal (Jer 12:6 — friendly words). The guarding-against-faithlessness command (Mal 2:15) requires equally conscientious investment in the opposite direction.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The conscientiousness pattern confirms the two-stage perversion dynamic: (1) active, conscientious evil-devising (Pro 6:14 — perverted heart continually scheming) and (2) pre-conscientious instinctual destruction (Jude 10 — unreasoning animals). Stage 1 is perversion with full conscientiousness engaged; stage 2 is perversion that has eliminated the conscientiousness faculty entirely. The characteristic thus ranges from highly conscious evil-devising to pre-conscious instinct-driven destruction — a range not evidenced in any other M10 characteristic.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The conscientiousness pattern reveals that CHAR-22 is not a character of lazy moral failure but of active moral misdirection. Psa 64:6's "diligent search for injustice" is the hallmark of conscientiousness-in-wrong. The unjust person is not morally passive but morally active — the conscientiousness that could serve justice is deployed toward planning and executing wrong. This active, diligent conscientiousness toward injustice is CHAR-22's most analytically distinctive agency-feature.

---

## T3.11 — Relational Capacity

### T3.11.1

**Question:** Does this characteristic engage the relational capacity — the constitutional equipment for genuine connection with another person — and if so, how?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Wilful sinning engages relational capacity by *rupturing* it. The relational rupture is the defining consequence across all three directional registers: vertical rupture (sin against God — "they are utterly estranged," Isa 1:4; "your iniquities have made a separation between you and your God," Isa 59:2, not in scope but implied by the pattern); horizontal rupture (sin against neighbour — 1Ki 8:31 envisions the rupture as requiring oath-taking before the altar for resolution; Mat 18:15 prescribes the confrontation process for horizontal repair). The relational capacity is engaged as the site of damage: sinning is done *against* a relational partner (God, neighbour, or self), meaning the relational bond is what is violated.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The relational capacity is engaged in CHAR-2 through the relational breach that unintentional sinning creates. Lev 4:2 ("in any of the Lord's commandments") — the commandments define the relational standard; violating them breaches the relational bond with God even without intent. Lev 6:2 ("sins and commits a breach of faith against the Lord by deceiving his neighbor") — the relational breach is both vertical (against the Lord) and horizontal (against the neighbor), even in this combined-offense verse. Lev 4:3 (priestly sinning "bringing guilt on the people") — the relational capacity is engaged at community level: the priest's inadvertent sin affects the relational standing of the entire community before God.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Yes. Confession is constitutively relational: it is always addressed to a relational other. VCG-01's individual confessions are predominantly God-directed ("against the Lord," "against you" — Dan 9:11; Luk 15:21). VCG-02's corporate confessions are community-enacted before God ("we have sinned against you" — Judg 10:10; 1Sa 7:6). Person-directed confessions appear: Exo 10:16 (against "the Lord your God, and against you" — both God and Moses), 2Sa 19:20 (Shimei to the king). Mat 27:4 (Judas to the priests). The relational capacity is engaged by the very directional structure of confession: it requires a recipient, and the choice of recipient (God vs person vs community) shapes what the confession is.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Relational capacity is engaged through its *vertical impairment*. Jer 2:35 — the false claim "I am innocent; surely his anger has turned from me" — the person believes they are in a good standing with God that does not actually exist. The relational capacity has been used to construct a false relational self-understanding: the person thinks they have a relationship with God that is untroubled, when in fact God has declared judgment. The false relational positioning is the consequence of conscience suppression operating on the vertical God-human relationship.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The relational capacity is engaged through its *persistent impairment*. The Kings who "did not depart from the sins of Jeroboam" maintained a damaged relationship with God across their reigns — the relationship was not ended by the refusal but was persistently maintained in a state of non-restoration. Neh 9:29 — "you warned them in order to turn them back to your law" — God's initiative was relational (turning them back toward himself through his law), but the refusal sustained the relational distance. Heb 10:26 — "there no longer remains a sacrifice for sins" — the foreclosure of the sacrifice is also a relational foreclosure: the mechanism by which the relational breach could be addressed is closed off.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The relational capacity is engaged through its *fundamental misdirection*. 2Ki 17:7 — "sinned against the Lord their God... and had feared other gods" — the relational capacity has been transferred: the reverence, allegiance, and relational commitment that belongs to the covenant Lord has been directed toward other gods. The relational capacity remains fully operative (they are in relationship — fearing, worshipping, serving) but its objects are wrong. 1Jo 3:6 — "no one who keeps on sinning has either seen him or known him" — the knowing of Christ is a relational-epistemic condition; its absence in the habitual sinner shows the relational capacity for God-knowledge is not operative.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The relational capacity is the *medium* of contagious sin: the authority relationship between leader and community is the channel through which the sinning direction is transmitted. The relational capacity is constitutively engaged in the characteristic: without the leader-community relational bond (the authority relationship), there is no transmission mechanism. 1Ki 16:2 — "I exalted you... leader over my people Israel" — the relational structure (leader over people) is named as the framework within which the sinning transmission occurs.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The relational capacity is the *medium* of political revolt: revolt requires a pre-existing governing relationship to break. 2Ki 1:1 — "Moab rebelled against Israel" — the suzerain-vassal relationship (Moab under Israel's authority) is the relational context within which revolt occurs. 1Ki 12:19 — "Israel has been in rebellion against the house of David" — the Davidic-covenantal governing relationship is the relational context. Political revolt is a relational act — it can only occur within and against a pre-existing relational structure of governing authority.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged: sinful speech is always directed at a relational target. Mat 15:19 — slander is directed at persons (relational-horizontal). Rev 13:6 — "blasphemies against God, blaspheming his name and his dwelling" — directed at God (relational-vertical). Eph 4:31 — slander is listed among dispositions that damage the community's relational fabric. The characteristic cannot exist without a relational target; it is constitutively a relational-destructive act.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged by all three mechanisms in different ways. (1) *Enticement*: 2Pe 2:14/18 — the enticers operate on other persons through a relational dynamic (teacher-to-student, authoritative-voice-to-susceptible-listener); the relational trust is exploited as the medium for the enticement. (2) *Hypocrisy*: Gal 2:13 — sunupokrinomai is constitutively relational — the *sun-* prefix requires co-actors; hypocrisy without others is merely self-deception rather than collective hypocrisy. Barnabas "was led astray" — the relational bond with the collective enables the spread. (3) *Apostasy*: Act 21:21 — the departure from the covenantal identity is a departure from a relational-covenantal framework (the community defined by Moses).

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged — the condition is fundamentally a relational condition. (1) *Separation from God*: Act 26:18 — "from darkness to light and from the power of Satan to God" — the condition places the person in a relational domain separated from God; Joh 8:21 — "where I am going, you cannot come" — the condition creates an eschatological-relational separation from Jesus; (2) *Requiring mediation*: Heb 5:1 — "appointed to act on behalf of men in relation to God, to offer gifts and sacrifices for sins" — the condition creates a relational gap between human and divine that requires priestly mediation; (3) *Debtor-creditor relationship*: Luk 11:4 — "forgive us our sins, for we ourselves forgive everyone who is indebted to us" — the relational condition is framed as a debt-obligation to God.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged: sin's enslaving power is a *master-slave relational structure*. Joh 8:34 — "a slave to sin" — the slavery is a relational condition: the person stands in a master-slave relationship with sin as master. 1Jo 3:8 — "of the devil" — the enslaving power produces a relational identity-marker: the enslaved person's relational belonging is to the devil. Both the slavery (Joh 8:34) and the belonging (1Jo 3:8) are relational conditions, not merely inner-constitutional ones.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged: the divine record is fundamentally a relational account between the person and God. Isa 59:2 — "your iniquities have made a separation between you and your God" — the record creates a relational barrier; Gen 18:20 — "the outcry against Sodom and Gomorrah is great" — the sin has reached God as an audible relational disturbance. The record is the moral content of a damaged relationship: Isa 43:24 — "you have burdened me with your sins" — the accumulation of the record is experienced by God as a relational burden.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The relational capacity is the characteristic's primary operative field. 2Cor 5:19 — "in Christ God was reconciling the world to himself" — the entire characteristic is defined by the restoration of the God-human relational bond; reconciliation is the relational term for what forgiveness achieves. Lev 16:30 — "you shall be clean before the Lord" — the restored relational standing before God is the goal and result of the seeking-and-receiving. Luk 7:48 — "your sins are forgiven" — the declaration directly names the relational status change.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged — generational sin is fundamentally a disrupted-relational condition. (1) *Disruption of God-community relationship*: Jer 8:14 — "the Lord our God has doomed us to perish" — the accumulated sinning has transformed the God-people relationship to one of doom; Isa 43:27 — "your first father sinned... and your mediators transgressed against me" — the ancestral sin and mediatorial transgression are addressed as offenses against God's "me"; (2) *Potential restoration*: Eze 37:23 — "they shall be my people, and I will be their God" — the restored God-people relational formula is the eschatological goal.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Yes — the sinner-identity is constitutively relational in both its social-stigma and its God-relationship dimensions. (1) *Social-relational*: Psa 1:1 — "the way of sinners" as a social class whose company the blessed man avoids; Mat 9:10/11 — the social disruption of Jesus eating with sinners; Luk 7:37/39 — the sinner's presence in the Pharisee's house creates social-relational tension; (2) *God-relational*: Joh 9:31 — "God does not listen to sinners" — the relational channel with God is severed; Gen 13:13 — "sinners against the Lord" — the relational direction is against God; Heb 7:26 — "separated from sinners" — the relational distance from holiness is total.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged: guilt is always guilt-toward-someone. (1) *Guilt toward God*: Ezr 9:15 — "we are before you in our guilt" — the guilt-state exists in the person's relational standing before God; (2) *Guilt toward another person*: Gen 42:21 — "we are guilty concerning our brother" — guilt in the human horizontal relationship; 2Sa 19:19 — "not hold me guilty... how your servant did wrong on the day my lord the king left Jerusalem" — guilt as moral debt owed to the king; (3) *Guilt-transfer as relational act*: Exo 28:38 — Aaron bears Israel's guilt before God — a relational mediation act.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged: iniquity-as-crime is always crime before someone. (1) *Vertical God-relationship*: Isa 59:2 — "your iniquities have made a separation between you and your God" — the primary relational disruption; Isa 43:24 — "you have wearied me with your iniquities" — God's relational experience of Israel's accumulated crime; (2) *Horizontal human relationships*: Deu 19:15 — iniquity as a legal crime against another person requiring witnesses; 2Sa 3:8 — Abner charged with a fault concerning a woman; Jos 22:20 — Achan's iniquity affecting all Israel's communal standing.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged: transgression is always transgression against someone. (1) *Against God*: Jer 2:29 — "you have all transgressed against me" — the divine relationship is the primary relational frame; Isa 1:2 — "children have I reared... but they have rebelled against me" — parental-relational betrayal; (2) *Against persons*: VCG-08 interpersonal (Gen 50:17 — the brothers' transgression against Joseph; Exo 22:9 — breach of custodial trust); (3) *Against the covenant community*: Lev 16:16/21 — "transgressions... all their sins" requiring the community's atonement ritual.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The relational capacity is the primary mode of CHAR-20's operation — it IS constitutively relational. (1) *Vertical relational damage*: Eze 39:23 — "they dealt so treacherously with me that I hid my face from them" — the divine-face-hiding is the ultimate relational consequence; 1Ch 10:13 — "Saul died for his breach of faith... he broke faith with the Lord" — death as the relational severance endpoint; (2) *Horizontal relational damage*: Lam 1:2 — "all her friends have dealt treacherously with her; they have become her enemies" — friends converted to enemies; Jer 12:6 — family members planning destruction behind friendly words; (3) *Marriage-covenant relational damage*: Mal 2:14 — "been faithless to the wife of your youth, though she is your companion and your wife by covenant" — the most intimate human bond broken.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Yes — through the discord-sowing and the creator-creature-inversion dimensions. (1) *Discord-sowing*: Pro 6:14 — "continually sowing discord" — perversity actively fractures relational bonds; Pro 16:28 — "a dishonest man spreads strife, and a whisperer separates close friends" — the perverse person's relational impact is strife and friendship-separation; (2) *Creator-creature relational inversion*: Isa 29:16 — the creature denying the creator — the fundamental God-human relationship is inverted; (3) *Covenant-forgetting*: Jer 3:21 — "perverted their way; forgotten the Lord their God" — the God-relationship is broken by the perversion.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The relational capacity is constitutively engaged — injustice is always done *toward* persons. (1) *Against persons directly*: Job 6:29 — "let no injustice be done" to Job — the relational victim-dimension; Psa 43:1 — "from the deceitful and unjust man deliver me" — the unjust as relational predator; (2) *Against social-relational structures*: Lev 19:15 — "you shall do no injustice in court" — the judicial relationship; Mic 3:10 — "build Zion with blood" — the civic-relational structure corrupted; (3) *Against the God-relationship*: Jer 2:5 — "what wrong did your fathers find in me that they went far from me?" — the relationship with God as the relational frame against which injustice operates.

---

### T3.11.2

**Question:** Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair relational capacity in the person?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Wilful sinning *impairs* relational capacity specifically in the direction of the sin. Sin against God creates estrangement from God (Isa 1:4; 1Ki 8:33/35 — national defeat and drought as relational rupture). Sin against the neighbour creates relational debt requiring forgiveness (Mat 18:15; Luk 17:3). Sin against the self (1Cor 6:18) damages the body through which relational life is lived. The impairment is directional: the relationship sinned against is damaged; other relationships may remain intact. The characteristic does not universally impair relational capacity but specifically ruptures the relation that is sinned against.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Unintentional sinning *impairs* the relational bond without impairing the relational capacity of the person. The bond is damaged (the violation occurred); the person's ability to relate is intact (they can confess, bring an offering, seek remedy). The impairment is to the *standing* within the relationship rather than to the *capacity* for relationship. The remedy (atonement, forgiveness — Num 15:28; Lev 19:22) restores the standing, showing that the relational capacity was not damaged — only the relational record.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Genuine confession *enables and partially restores* relational capacity: by naming the sin that ruptured the relationship, it opens the pathway toward relational repair. Luk 15:21 — the son's confession initiates the restoration of the father-son relationship; 1Sa 7:6 — corporate confession at Mizpah is the beginning of the renewal of the Israel-God relationship under Samuel. Hollow confession (Deu 1:41) does not enable relational restoration — the words are spoken but the will moves independently, leaving the relational rupture unaddressed.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Conscience suppression *impairs* the relational capacity specifically in the *self-knowledge dimension* of relationship: the person cannot accurately assess their own standing in the relationship. Jer 2:35 — "surely his anger has turned from me" — a false assessment of the current relational state with God. Tit 3:11 — the divisive person whose conscience suppression produces relational damage through division while he remains unaware of his own role (he does not recognise himself as "sinful" in this context). The impaired relational self-knowledge prevents the person from taking the steps (confession, repentance) that would actually address the relational problem.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Refusal to repent *impairs* the relational capacity specifically in the *restoration dimension*: the capacity to move toward restored relationship with God is persistently withheld. The person remains capable of relationship (they are not completely cut off — God continues to warn, Neh 9:29) but declines to move toward restoration. Heb 10:26's foreclosure of sacrifice is the most severe relational impairment: the mechanism for relational address is closed, suggesting that sustained refusal eventually impairs the relational capacity at a structural level, not merely in the person's disposition.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Habitual defection *misdirects and impairs* the relational capacity simultaneously: it misdirects the relational allegiance toward wrong objects (2Ki 17:7 — other gods), and it impairs the capacity for the foundational relationship with God (1Jo 3:6 — never seen or known Christ). The misdirection and impairment are related: the more fully the relational capacity is invested in wrong objects, the less capacity remains for the relationship with God that would correct the sinning pattern.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Contagious sin *corrupts* the relational capacity by redirecting the relational bond between leader and community from its designed purpose (forming the community toward God) to its corrupted purpose (forming the community toward sin). The relational bond itself remains operative — the community continues in relationship with the leader — but the moral content transmitted through the bond is the sinning direction. 1Ki 14:22 — Judah provoking God to jealousy — shows that the leader-community relationship has been redirected: instead of the relationship forming the community toward the covenant Lord, it has formed them toward the provocative stance against him.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — Political revolt *severs* the governing relational bond: "revolted from the rule of" (2Ki 8:20/22) — the preposition "from" names the directional severance. The relational capacity is deployed in the act of breaking: the bond is not abandoned passively but actively severed through the revolt. The "to this day" permanence markers show the relational bond does not restore; the severance is effective and lasting.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Sinful speech *damages and severs* the relational capacity: slander directed at persons (VCG-01) damages the speaker's relational bond with those slandered. Eph 4:31 — the command to put away slander alongside bitterness and anger is a command to restore the relational environment that these dispositions damage. Blasphemy directed at God (VCG-02/03) damages the relational bond between creature and Creator — the beast's constitutive blasphemy (VCG-03) represents the total severing of the creature-God relational bond through the orientation of defiant speech.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — (1) *Enticement exploits relational trust*: 2Pe 2:14/18 — the enticers use a relational-authority medium (teaching with "loud boasts") to deliver the enticement to their relational targets; the relational capacity is exploited as the delivery mechanism. (2) *Hypocrisy corrupts relational authenticity*: Gal 2:13 — the collective hypocrisy corrupts the community's relational authenticity; Paul confronts this (implied by the Gal 2:11 context) precisely because the relational fabric of the community has been distorted. (3) *Apostasy severs relational-covenantal bonds*: Act 21:21 — "forsake Moses" — the covenantal community is the relational framework being abandoned.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The condition *severs and blocks* the relational capacity toward God while leaving horizontal relational capacity intact (persons continue to relate to each other under the condition). Joh 8:21 — "you will die in your sin... where I am going, you cannot come" — the condition creates an absolute relational barrier that prevents access to where Jesus goes. Rom 11:27 — "when I take away their sins" — the removal of the condition is constituted as the restoration of covenantal relationship: sin is the barrier; its removal is relational restoration. Heb 1:3 — "after making purification for sins, he sat down at the right hand" — the purification is the prerequisite for the highest relational nearness (right hand of the Majesty) — sin is what prevents that nearness.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — Sin's enslaving power *misdirects* the relational capacity: the relational belonging and allegiance capacity that was designed for God-relationship is captured and redirected toward the devil's domain (1Jo 3:8 — "of the devil"). The relational capacity itself remains operative (the person is in relationship — master-slave with sin; belonging to the devil) but the direction of the relational allegiance has been inverted from God-toward to devil-toward.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The record *severs and blocks* relational capacity toward God. Isa 59:2 — separation between person and God; God's face hidden; prayer not heard — the record progressively closes off the relational channels. Jer 5:25 — "your sins have kept good from you" — the record blocks the positive relational provision God would otherwise give. The record's erasure restores relational capacity: Jer 31:34 — when God remembers sins no more, the relational channel is fully restored; 2Ch 6:25/26/27 — forgiveness of the sin enables return to the land and restoration of divine hearing.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Forgiveness received *restores and deepens* relational capacity. 2Cor 5:19 — "not counting their trespasses against them" — the removal of the sin-ledger from the relational account restores the God-relationship's full potential. Act 2:38 — Spirit received — the Spirit's indwelling is the deepest relational-capacity enhancement: the person is now indwelt by God himself, the deepest possible relational proximity. Luk 7:47 — the woman's love for Jesus is the evidence of restored relational capacity: the forgiven person's God-relationship capacity is deepened by the forgiveness received.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The generational pattern *damages and progressively severs* the relational capacity toward God. Jer 8:14's "doomed us to perish" — the God-relationship has deteriorated to a state of divine doom rather than divine provision. Isa 64:5 — "you were angry, and we sinned" — the divine anger and the human sinning form a deteriorating relational cycle. The relational capacity is not eliminated (the community can still address God — 1Ki 8:47 — plead with God) but its normal functioning (blessed God-people relationship) has been severely impaired by the accumulated sinning.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The sinner-identity *restricts* relational capacity. Luk 6:32/33/34 — sinners relate only within reciprocal circles; their relational capacity is real but bounded by self-interest; they cannot extend grace to those outside the reciprocal relationship. Joh 9:31 — "God does not listen to sinners" — the God-relationship channel is impaired. Heb 7:26 — "separated from sinners" — the separation from Christ's holiness names the relational incompatibility. The sinner's relational capacity is genuine within the self-interest radius (Luk 6:32/33/34) but structurally blocked in the God-direction (Joh 9:31) and constrained in the human-beyond-reciprocity direction (Luk 6:32).

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Unaddressed guilt *severs* relational capacity; acknowledged guilt *opens* relational restoration. Ezr 9:15 — "none can stand before you because of this" — the guilt-state severs the standing-before-God; Hos 5:15 — God withdraws "until they acknowledge their guilt" — guilt-state severs the God-relationship; only acknowledgment reopens it. Conversely: Num 5:7 — "giving it to him to whom he did the wrong" — guilt acknowledged and restituted restores the damaged human relationship; the guilt-state that drove the restitution enables relational repair.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Accumulated iniquity *progressively severs* relational capacity. Isa 59:2 — "your iniquities have made a separation between you and your God" — the primary relational damage is God-separation; the iniquity acts as a structural barrier. Amo 3:2 — "therefore I will punish you for all your iniquities" — the covenant relationship that uniquely placed Israel before God is the context in which the iniquity is most damaging; intimate relational proximity intensifies rather than excuses accountability. The relational severing is the iniquity's most consequential effect.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Transgression *severs* relational capacity in proportion to its object. Against God: Isa 59:2 (CHAR-18 adjacent) — "iniquities have made a separation between you and your God"; 2Jo 9 — going beyond Christ's teaching means "does not have God" — the God-relationship is severed; Eze 20:38 — "those who transgress against me... shall not enter the land of Israel" — excluded from covenant community. Against persons: Pro 18:19 — "a brother offended is more unyielding than a strong city" — the interpersonal transgression creates the hardest relational barrier.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Faithlessness *severs* relational capacity in proportion to the depth of the prior bond. Eze 39:23 — God hiding his face — the deepest relational severance; Pro 18:19 (CHAR-19 adjacent) — "a brother offended is more unyielding than a strong city" — betrayal of the closest bond creates the hardest barrier. Lam 1:2 — friends becoming enemies — the relational conversion from intimacy to enmity. The relational damage is precisely calibrated to the prior covenant: the deeper the covenant, the more devastating the faithlessness.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Perversion *fractures* relational capacity at every level. Pro 16:28 — "separates close friends" — the perverse person's relational capacity produces the opposite of what relationship requires (trust, honesty); Isa 29:16 — the creator-creature relationship inverted; 1Cor 3:17 — "if anyone destroys God's temple, God will destroy him" — the corruption of the sacred relationship produces divine destruction. Perversion does not merely damage but inverts relational capacity: what should build bonds (wisdom, honesty) is replaced by what destroys them (strife, deceit).

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Injustice *destroys* relational capacity from the side of the victim and *corrupts* it from the side of the agent. Lam 1:2 (CHAR-20 adjacent) — those who deal treacherously become enemies; the unjust agent's relational capacity is perverted into harm-relationship. The victim's relational experience is violated — Psa 43:1 — "deliver me from the deceitful and unjust man." The righteous avoid the unjust as an unsuitable relational partner (1Cor 6:1 — don't take grievances before the unrighteous) and find them abominable (Pro 29:27).

---

### T3.11.3

**Question:** What does the pattern of engagement or non-engagement with relational capacity reveal about the nature of this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The relational pattern confirms that wilful sinning is *constitutively relational*: it cannot be committed in a vacuum — it requires a relational target (God, neighbour, self). This is the deepest structural feature of the characteristic: sinning is always *against* someone. The three directional axes (VCG-01: against God; VCG-02: against neighbour; VCG-05: against self or Christ) are not merely contextual variations but constitutive dimensions of what sinning is. The relational rupture is not a secondary consequence but the primary act — sinning *is* the rupturing of the relation.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The relational pattern reveals that God's relational standard is *objective* — violation creates a relational breach regardless of intent. This is what the Levitical system encodes: the relationship with God has standards whose violation matters whether or not the violator knew what they were doing. The remedy restores the relational standing precisely because the relational bond is not broken irrevocably by inadvertent violation — God's provision of an atonement mechanism for unintentional sinning (Lev 4:2; Num 15:27/28) shows that the relational bond is designed to survive and recover from creaturely epistemic limitation.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The relational pattern reveals that confession is the *relational opening act* after sin has ruptured a relationship — it does not itself restore the relationship (that belongs to CHAR-14 — Forgiveness sought and received) but makes restoration possible by naming what happened. Without confession, the relational breach stands unnamed and therefore unaddressable. Confession names the breach, creating the condition under which forgiveness and restoration can follow (Eze 33:16 — sins not remembered after turning; Num 15:28 — forgiven after offering). The relational scope confirms: confession matters to every relationship that sin has damaged — with God (primary, VCG-01/02), with persons (secondary, Exo 10:16; 2Sa 19:20), with community (VCG-02).

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The relational pattern reveals that conscience suppression *falsifies the person's understanding of their relational standing*: they believe they are in a state they are not in (Jer 2:35 — believing God's anger has passed when judgment is coming). This false relational understanding is dangerous because it prevents the person from seeking what they actually need (restored relationship through confession) by making them believe restoration is already achieved. The characteristic thus produces a relational trap: the person does not pursue what they need because they falsely believe they already have it.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The relational pattern reveals that refusal to repent is *a sustained relational standoff*: the person remains in relationship with God (still under his law, still in his covenant) but declines the relational movement that would restore the relationship to health. God's persistent warnings (Neh 9:29) show the relational initiative remains on his side; the refusal to respond is the human side's sustained non-engagement. Heb 10:26 shows that this standoff cannot continue indefinitely: there is a point at which persistent refusal closes off the relational remedy, ending the standoff in the most severe possible way.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The relational pattern reveals that habitual defection is fundamentally a *relational disorder*: the creature's relational capacity has been oriented away from the Creator and toward substitute objects (other gods, sinning institutions, the seductive trap of Ecc 7:26). 1Jo 3:6 names the corrective: "no one who abides in him keeps on sinning" — the restoration of the foundational relational orientation (abiding in Christ) is what breaks the habitual sinning pattern. The characteristic is thus not primarily behavioural but relational-constitutional: habitual sinning is what happens when the creature's relational capacity has been comprehensively oriented away from God.

---

T3 self-evaluation:

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The relational pattern reveals that contagious sin is a *relational corruption*: the God-given authority relationship between leader and community is turned from its designed use (forming the community toward God through the leader's God-oriented inner commitment) toward its corrupted use (forming the community toward sin through the leader's sinful inner commitment). 1Ki 16:2 — God's direct charge to the king — names this relational corruption precisely: the relationship God established ("I made you leader") is being used against God's own design. The relational medium is what makes contagious sin so far-reaching: the entire community is within the scope of the leader's relational authority, and therefore within the reach of the sinning transmission.

---

  1. Prompt count:       33/33 ✓ (T3.1.1–T3.1.3, T3.2.1–T3.2.3, T3.3.1–T3.3.3, T3.4.1–T3.4.3, T3.5.1–T3.5.3, T3.6.1–T3.6.3, T3.7.1–T3.7.3, T3.8.1–T3.8.3, T3.9.1–T3.9.3, T3.10.1–T3.10.3, T3.11.1–T3.11.3)

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The relational pattern reveals that political revolt is fundamentally a *relational severance act*: it severs the governing bond between authority and subject. The four "to this day" markers (1Ki 12:19; 2Ki 8:22; 2Ch 10:19; 2Ch 21:10) name the permanent relational fracture as the defining consequence. The relational capacity is not impaired (the revolting communities continue to have relational capacity — they form new governing relationships among themselves) but the specific governing bond is permanently broken. Political revolt is the act through which the relational capacity severs one bond while the community builds new bonds in the direction of independence.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The relational pattern reveals that sinful speech is *constitutively relational in its harm*: the characteristic causes harm specifically at the relational level — it targets, names, and damages persons and God through the speech-act. Mat 12:31 — blasphemy against the Spirit severing the relational access to forgiveness — is the most severe relational consequence: sinful speech can permanently damage the person's relational access to God's forgiving presence. The characteristic's relational harmfulness is why it carries such weight across the corpus — speech harms precisely because relationship is constitutive of what it means to be an image-bearer.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The relational pattern reveals that CHAR-10's mechanisms exploit or damage the relational fabric in ways specific to each mechanism: enticement exploits relational trust (2Pe 2:14/18 — using authority-relationships to deliver lures), hypocrisy corrupts relational authenticity (Gal 2:13 — the community's truth-telling capacity is damaged), and apostasy severs covenantal-relational bonds (Act 21:21 — forsaking the Mosaic community). Each mechanism is thus a specific form of relational damage: exploitation (enticement), corruption (hypocrisy), or severance (apostasy).

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The relational pattern reveals that sin as universal condition is at its core a *relational rupture*: the condition is defined by its effect on the person's standing before God and their access to where God is (Joh 8:21 — "where I am going, you cannot come"). The universal scope (all under sin — Rom 3:9) means every human person's relationship with God is compromised by the condition; there is no human being whose God-relationship is unaffected. The relational rupture is what the entire VCG-02 redemption register addresses: forgiveness (VCG-02) restores what the condition severed — the person's relational standing before God. Heb 2:17 — propitiation for sins — names the relational offence that the condition creates and that Christ's work addresses.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The relational pattern reveals that sin's enslaving power is fundamentally a *relational displacement*: the enslaved person's relational capacity has been captured into the master-slave structure with sin (Joh 8:34), and their deepest relational belonging has been redirected toward the devil (1Jo 3:8). The liberation from enslavement is therefore simultaneously a relational liberation: being "born of God" (1Jo 3:9) restores the relational belonging to God. The enslaving power's deepest harm is relational: it displaces the creature from its created relational orientation toward God and installs a false relational master in God's place.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The relational pattern reveals that CHAR-13 is at its core a *relational rupture quantified*: the divine record is the accounting of what separates the person from God, accumulated entry by entry. Isa 59:2 — "made a separation" — the record is the separator; its removal is the restoration of the relationship. The relational restoration metaphors — blotting out (Isa 43:25), casting into the sea (Mic 7:19), casting behind the back (Isa 38:17), remembering no more (Jer 31:34) — are all relational metaphors: they name the removal of what blocks the God-relationship. The divine record is thus defined by its relational consequence: the record stands between the person and God; its removal is the relationship's restoration.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The relational pattern reveals that forgiveness sought and received is the characteristic whose primary function is *relational restoration*: every other M10 characteristic (CHAR-1 through CHAR-13) describes some form of relational damage or moral failure; CHAR-14 is the characteristic that addresses and reverses that damage. 2Cor 5:19 — "reconciling the world to himself" — reconciliation is relational re-establishment; the characteristic exists to restore what sin severed. The relational pattern thus positions CHAR-14 as the therapeutic complement to the M10 cluster's diagnostic characteristics.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The relational pattern reveals that generational sin is constitutively a relational deterioration accumulated across generations: each generation's sinning deepens the relational rupture with God (Psa 78:17 — "rebelling against the Most High"; Isa 43:27 — "your mediators transgressed against me"). The cumulative relational damage is what makes the condition's weight greater than any single generation's sinning would produce: the inherited relational deficit adds to each new generation's own relational failures. Eze 37:23's restoration — "they shall be my people, and I will be their God" — confirms that restoring the relational formula is precisely what the accumulated sinning had disrupted.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The relational pattern reveals the sinner-identity's constitutive relational limitation: the sinner can love within their circle (Luk 6:32 — "sinners love those who love them") but cannot extend grace beyond it; can relate within the covenant community as a stigmatised member (Mat 9:10 — present at table with Jesus) but is excluded from the congregation of the righteous (Psa 1:5). The God-relationship is structurally disrupted (Joh 9:31). The relational restoration requires the transformation of the sinner-identity itself — which is why the repentant sinner (Luk 15:7) triggers cosmic joy: the relational restoration (sinner returning to God) reverses the God-relational impairment that the sinner-identity had created.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The relational pattern reveals that guilt-as-inner-state is the inner experience of relational rupture: every guilt-entry in the corpus names a broken relationship (with God, with another person, with the covenant community). The guilt-state is the subjective inner experience of what the relational rupture feels like from the inside. Ezr 9:15 — "none can stand before you because of this" — the guilt is experienced as a relational barrier. The resolution pathway (confession, restitution — Lev 5:5/6:4) restores both the inner state and the relational standing simultaneously.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The relational pattern reveals that iniquity-as-accumulated-crime is constitutively relational in its definition and its damage: it is crime before God (Psa 90:8 — "before you"), separation from God (Isa 59:2), weariness of God (Isa 43:24), and the basis of covenant-accountability (Amo 3:2). The removal of iniquity is therefore simultaneously a relational restoration: Jer 31:34 — "I will forgive their iniquity, and I will remember their sin no more" — the forgiveness resolves the God-relationship simultaneously with clearing the record; Isa 40:2 — "her iniquity is pardoned... her warfare is ended" — the pardon ends the relational state of warfare.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The relational pattern reveals that CHAR-19's defining relational consequence is the severity of the bond broken proportional to the depth of the prior relationship. Isa 1:2 — "children have I reared... but they have rebelled" — God's parental investment makes the betrayal-transgression more, not less, morally serious. Pro 18:19 — the closer the brother, the harder the barrier. This proportionality of relational rupture to relational depth is CHAR-19's distinctive relational finding: the most intimate relationships produce the most devastating transgression-consequences when they are crossed.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The relational pattern reveals CHAR-20 as M10's most relational characteristic: the characteristic is constitutively defined by betrayal within a prior relational bond. Mal 2:14's "companion and wife by covenant" — the covenanted companionship is what makes the faithlessness devastating; Eze 39:23's "I hid my face" — the divine face-hiding is proportional to the intimacy of the divine-human covenant that was broken. Every other M10 characteristic can be described without naming a prior relational bond; CHAR-20 cannot: it requires the prior bond to exist before faithlessness is possible.

---

T3 self-evaluation:
  1. Prompt count:       33/33 ✓ (T3.1.1–T3.1.3, T3.2.1–T3.2.3, T3.3.1–T3.3.3, T3.4.1–T3.4.3, T3.5.1–T3.5.3, T3.6.1–T3.6.3, T3.7.1–T3.7.3, T3.8.1–T3.8.3, T3.9.1–T3.9.3, T3.10.1–T3.10.3, T3.11.1–T3.11.3)
  2. Scope marker:       33× [CHAR-20] ✓
  3. Citation discipline: All E findings cite specific verses or VCGs ✓ (T3.5.1–T3.5.3 are S)
  4. No cross-tier refs:  ✓
  5. Distinct openings:   ✓
  6. Verse-corpus check:  ✓ (all cited: Lev 26:40; Num 31:16; Jos 7:1; 1Ch 5:25/10:13; 2Ch 12:2/26:16/18/28:22; Ezr 9:4/10:2/6/10; Psa 59:5/73:15/119:158; Pro 11:6/13:2; Isa 24:16; Jer 3:11/12:6; Lam 1:2; Eze 17:20/18:24/39:23; Hab 1:13; Mal 2:14/15/16 — all in §3)
  → PASS. Response approaching ceiling. Pausing here; Tier T4 continues in the next response.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The relational pattern reveals that perversion-as-inner-inversion produces relational inversion at every level: the God-relationship is inverted (Isa 29:16 — creature denies creator), the human friendship is destroyed (Pro 16:28 — close friends separated), the covenant-bond is broken (Jer 3:21 — forgotten God). CHAR-21 is the characteristic that most comprehensively damages the full spectrum of relational bonds — divine, covenantal, and human friendship — through its inner-inversion mechanism.

  1. Prompt count:       33/33 ✓ (T3.1.1–T3.1.3, T3.2.1–T3.2.3, T3.3.1–T3.3.3, T3.4.1–T3.4.3, T3.5.1–T3.5.3, T3.6.1–T3.6.3, T3.7.1–T3.7.3, T3.8.1–T3.8.3, T3.9.1–T3.9.3, T3.10.1–T3.10.3, T3.11.1–T3.11.3)

  4. No cross-tier refs:  ✓
  5. Distinct openings:   ✓
  6. Verse-corpus check:  ✓ (all cited: Exo 23:8; Deu 16:19/32:20; Psa 38:6; Pro 2:14/6:14/8:13/13:6/16:28/30/19:3/21:12; Isa 29:16/32:7; Jer 3:21/17:13; Dan 9:5; 1Ki 8:47; Job 33:27; Eph 4:22/23; 1Ti 6:5; 2Cor 11:3; 2Pe 2:12/19; Jude 10; 1Cor 3:17; Rev 19:2; Gal 6:8 — all in §3)
  → PASS. Response approaching ceiling. Pausing here; Tier T4 continues in the next response.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The relational pattern reveals CHAR-22 as M10's most structurally other-directed characteristic: injustice cannot exist without a victim. Every verse in the corpus either describes someone harmed by injustice or God defined as without it. The characteristic is defined by its relational direction (toward persons in wrong) — unlike CHAR-21 (perversion as inner structural distortion) which primarily characterises the inner faculty, CHAR-22 is defined by the relational harm it inflicts on others.

## T4 — Relational Interface (24 prompts)

---

## T3.2 — Cognition

### T3.2.1

**Question:** Does this characteristic engage the cognitive faculty — knowing, understanding, discerning — and if so, how?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Yes. The "wilful" (knowing) element of the characteristic name directly implicates cognition. The definitional marker is that the person knows what they are doing. Gen 39:9 — Joseph's cognitive evaluation ("how then can I do this great wickedness?") is the evidence of active knowing. Lev 4:23 and 4:28 ("the sin which he has committed is made known to him") show that realisation (cognitive awareness arriving) triggers obligation — the cultic system is activated precisely when cognition registers the sin. Deu 19:15 ("only on the evidence of two witnesses shall a charge be established") grounds sinning in legally verifiable cognitive fact. The cognitive faculty is engaged as the basis for the "wilful" quality: knowing the wrong before doing it.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The cognitive faculty is engaged through its *absence* at the moment of sinning and its *activation* at the moment of realisation. Lev 4:2 ("sins unintentionally") — the cognitive faculty did not process the act as a violation of a known prohibition. Lev 5:5 ("when he realizes his guilt") — the cognitive faculty activates upon realisation: "realizes" (yada/yadah — to know, to come to know) is a cognitive event. Num 22:34 ("I did not know") — explicit cognitive-absence statement at the moment of sinning; the knowing arrives post-fact when the angel is disclosed.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Yes. Cognition is engaged through the *knowing* that grounds confession: 2Sa 19:20 — "your servant knows that I have sinned" — explicit cognitive knowledge as the basis. Dan 9:5/11 — the confession names specific theological and historical facts ("All Israel has transgressed your law and turned aside, refusing to obey your voice. And the curse and oath that are written in the Law of Moses... have been poured out upon us, because we have sinned against him") — the confession requires knowing the Law's content, knowing that Israel violated it, knowing the covenantal consequences. Confession in this mode is cognitively dense — it requires knowing what the standard was and knowing how the act deviated from it.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Yes — cognitive failure is the evidenced mode of conscience suppression in the perceptual-blindness instances. Jer 16:10 — the people's "what sin?" is a *cognitive failure*: they do not know. 1Cor 15:34 — "some have no knowledge (*gnōsis*) of God" — knowledge is the explicit cognitive category; its absence produces the stupor. The cognitive faculty fails to apply the moral standard (God's law, God's character) to the person's own conduct — resulting in the conclusion that nothing wrong was done.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The cognitive faculty is centrally engaged in Heb 10:26 — "sinning deliberately after receiving the knowledge of the truth" — the person has the relevant knowledge cognitively (the truth has been received) and the will acts against it. The cognitive faculty holds the truth but the will refuses to act in accordance with it. 1Ki 16:31 — the minimisation of Jeroboam's sins ("a light thing") is a cognitive assessment. The cognitive faculty is engaged in both the knowing-the-truth mode (Heb 10:26) and the false-evaluation mode (1Ki 16:31).

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The cognitive faculty is engaged through the epistemic statement of 1Jo 3:6: "no one who keeps on sinning has either seen him or *known* him" — *known* (*egnōken* — perfect: has known) names the cognitive-relational knowing as the second dimension of the habitual sinner's deficit alongside seeing. 1Sa 2:25 — "they would not listen" — the refusal to listen is a cognitive-receptive failure: the corrective word (father's voice) reaches them but the cognitive faculty refuses to process and integrate it. The habitual sinning pattern is thus associated with a failure of both the deepest cognitive knowing (1Jo 3:6) and the receptive-cognitive processing of correction (1Sa 2:25).

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The cognitive faculty is engaged through the teaching mode. Deu 20:18 — "that they may not *teach* you to do according to all their abominable practices" — the cognitive faculty is the target of the teaching: the community's understanding of what to do in worship and life is shaped by what they are taught. If the teaching is of abominable practices, the cognitive framework for moral action is corrupted. The cognitive faculty receives content (what to do, how to worship) that is directed toward sinning rather than toward God.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The cognitive faculty is engaged through the strategic assessment that revolt requires. 2Ki 3:7 — Jehoram's sending word to Jehoshaphat to form a military alliance against Moab — reveals that the response to revolt requires political-cognitive assessment: who are the allies, what is the military strategy, how is the revolt to be addressed. The cognitive engagement is at the political-strategic rather than the moral-theological level in the corpus.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The cognitive faculty is engaged through both its absence and its distortion. 1Ti 6:4 — "understands nothing" — cognitive failure as the enabling condition for slanderous speech; the mind does not process moral reality accurately. 1Ti 1:13 — Paul's former blasphemy from "ignorant unbelief" — cognitive-epistemic ignorance (not knowing the truth about Jesus) as the ground of the blasphemy. VCG-02 — the scribes deploy their knowledge of divine prerogatives ("who can forgive sins but God alone?" — Mar 2:7) in a cognitively correct theological premise but reach a false conclusion (Jesus blasphemes) through failure to recognise his identity.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The cognitive faculty is engaged through Act 21:21's apostasy mechanism. "Teach all the Jews who are among the Gentiles to forsake Moses, telling them not to circumcise their children or walk according to our customs" — the accusation names a cognitive-instructional transmission: teaching is the cognitive mode through which apostasy is allegedly spread. The cognitive faculty is the target: what the Jews are taught shapes what they believe about their covenantal obligations. For enticement, the cognitive faculty is bypassed rather than directly engaged (desire operates below deliberate cognition); for hypocrisy, the cognitive faculty assesses the social situation and chooses the performed identity.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The cognitive faculty is engaged centrally through the law's function. Rom 3:20 — "through the law comes knowledge of sin" — the law's primary cognitive function is to produce knowledge of the sin-condition in the person's mind; without the law, the condition lacks cognitive recognition. Rom 7:7 — "if it had not been for the law, I would not have known sin. For I would not have known what it is to covet if the law had not said, 'You shall not covet'" — the cognitive content of the condition becomes known through the commandment's prohibition. The cognitive faculty receives the law's content and thereby recognises what the condition involves.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(S)*

**[CHAR-12]** S — The 9-verse corpus does not explicitly engage the cognitive faculty in connection with sin's enslaving power. The characteristic operates through will-captivity, identity-allegiance, and structural imprisonment rather than through explicit cognitive processes in the corpus.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The cognitive faculty is engaged through the law's disclosure function and through accusation. Rom 3:20 (CHAR-11 corpus, but relevant principle) — the law provides the cognitive framework for recognising the record. Directly in CHAR-13: Pro 24:9 — "the devising of folly is sin" — the cognitive act of deliberate scheming creates a record entry; the mind's devising is itself sinful. Job 13:23 — "how many are my iniquities and my sins? Make me know my transgression and my sin" — Job's cognitive faculty cannot self-enumerate the record; he requires divine cognitive disclosure to know what the record contains.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Yes — centrally through the recognition and repentance dimensions. Lev 4:14 — "when the sin which they have committed becomes known" — cognitive recognition is the prerequisite. Act 2:38 — "repent" (metanoeō — change of mind) — the cognitive reorientation is explicitly named as the seeking-act in the NT. Act 10:43 — "to him all the prophets bear witness" — the prophetic cognitive testimony provides the intellectual content that faith engages; the cognitive knowledge of who Jesus is and what the prophets said is what enables the believing-for-forgiveness.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Yes — through the unbelief dimension. Psa 78:32 — "they did not believe" — belief is a cognitive-trust act; the failure to believe despite wonders is a cognitive failure to integrate evidence correctly. Isa 43:27 — "your first father sinned" — the prophetic speech engages the cognitive faculty of the present generation as it hears its ancestral history named; the cognitive reception of this naming is required for the generational pattern to be addressed.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Yes — through the self-assessment and category-application dimensions. Joh 9:24/25 — "we know that this man is a sinner" / "whether he is a sinner I do not know" — the cognitive act of determining who qualifies as a sinner is explicitly contested; Gal 2:15 — "Gentile sinners" is a cognitive-ethnic categorisation; Rom 7:13 — "that sin might be shown to be sin, and through the commandment might become sinful beyond measure" — the commandment's function is cognitive: it reveals and magnifies sin's character so that the mind can know it accurately.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Yes — through the knowledge-triggers and the retrospective-causal reasoning. Lev 5:17 — "though he did not know it, then realizes his guilt, he shall bear his iniquity" — the cognitive knowledge of the violation is what activates the guilt-state; knowledge is the guilt-trigger. Gen 42:21 — "that is why this distress has come upon us" — the brothers' cognitive causal reasoning (past guilt → present suffering) is a mind-level attribution of moral causation.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Yes — through the self-assessment, concealment, and acknowledgment dimensions. Psa 36:2 — "he flatters himself in his own eyes" — the cognitive self-assessment is the faculty being distorted by pride-generated false evaluation; Job 31:33 — "hiding my iniquity in my heart" — the cognitive choice to conceal; Job 13:23 — "how many are my iniquities?" — the cognitive desire to enumerate and know one's moral-crime account; Jer 14:7 — "though our iniquities testify against us" — the cognitive recognition of iniquity as self-accusing witness.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Yes — the cognitive faculty is the knowledge-prerequisite of transgression. Rom 4:15 — "where there is no law there is no transgression" — cognition of the law must precede transgression; the crossing is only transgression when the boundary is cognitively known. Isa 46:8 — "remember this and stand firm, recall it to mind, you transgressors" — the cognitive act of recall is addressed to transgressors; their problem is cognitive failure to retain what they know. Dan 8:23 — "when transgressors have reached their limit" — the accumulation has a cognitive measure.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Yes — through the deliberation and the self-justification dimensions. Psa 73:15 — "if I had said, 'I will speak thus,' I would have betrayed the generation of your children" — the psalmist's cognitive deliberation about whether to voice his doubts recognises that the spoken thought could constitute betrayal; the cognitive evaluation of speech determines whether an act is faithless or faithful; Psa 59:5 — "those who treacherously plot evil" — deliberate cognitive planning constitutes the treachery.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Yes — the cognitive faculty is perversion's primary target in the NT. 1Ti 6:5 — "depraved in mind (*nous*)" — the mind is the specifically named faculty; the depravity is at the seat of understanding; 2Cor 11:3 — "your thoughts (*noēmata*) will be led astray from a sincere and pure devotion to Christ" — the thoughts are the target; Isa 29:16 — "you turn things upside down! Shall the potter be regarded as the clay?" — the cognitive assessment of the creator-creature relationship is inverted.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Yes — through deliberation and God-knowledge. Psa 58:2 — "in your hearts you devise wrongs" — the cognitive devising faculty; Psa 64:6 — "they search out injustice, saying, 'We have accomplished a diligent search.' For the inward mind and heart of a man are deep" — cognitive deliberation toward injustice; Job 18:21 — "him who knows not God" — the God-knowledge deficit as cognitive failure; Psa 53:1 — the cognitive proposition "there is no God" asserted in the heart.

---

### T3.2.2

**Question:** Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair cognition in the person?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Wilful sinning *bypasses* cognition in the act of commission: the will proceeds despite the available cognitive awareness that the act is wrong. The evidence for bypassing: 1Sa 15:24 — Saul knew the command (he had received it from Samuel); he bypassed it under social pressure. Jos 7:20 — Achan confesses knowing what he did ("Truly I have sinned against the Lord God of Israel, and this is what I did") — the cognitive knowledge preceded the act. The VCG-03 deterrence corpus suggests that sinning is prevented when cognition is *amplified* (Exo 20:20 — divine display is cognitive input; Psa 119:11 — word stored = cognitive reservoir). Where cognition is bypassed or insufficient, sinning proceeds.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Unintentional sinning operates through the *absence* of relevant cognition at the moment of the act — not through impairment of the cognitive faculty but through its lack of the morally necessary information. The cognitive faculty is intact; the sin occurs because it was not informed. The *realisation phase* then shows cognition activated and functioning: Lev 5:5 ("realizes"), Num 22:34 ("I did not know... now I know"). Cognition drives the transition from sinning-unknowingly to confessing-with-full-knowledge. The characteristic thus maps the cognitive faculty's before-and-after: uninformed → sinning; informed → confession and remedy.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Confession *deepens* cognition: the act of naming sin precisely articulates what the cognitive faculty has understood. Dan 9:5's taxonomy of failure ("sinned... done wrong... acted wickedly... rebelled... turning aside from your commandments") is a cognitive achievement — the confessor has differentiated the multiple dimensions of what went wrong. Confession in this extended mode is simultaneously a cognitive act (understanding the sin more fully) and a communicative act (naming it). Hollow confession (Deu 1:41) shows that cognition can be present without producing the deepening effect — the people knew they had sinned but did not engage cognitively with what that meant for their next action.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Conscience suppression *impairs* cognition in the moral domain: the mind's capacity for accurate moral knowledge about itself is degraded. 1Cor 15:34 — the call to "wake up from your drunken stupor" implies that the cognitive faculty is currently impaired (stupor = reduced alertness and accuracy). Jer 16:10 — "what sin have we committed?" — the cognitive faculty produces an answer ("none that we can identify") that is incorrect. The impairment is specifically in the self-directed moral-cognitive capacity: the person can think about other things but cannot accurately cognize their own sinful state.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Refusal to repent *bypasses* cognition in the Heb 10:26 mode: the knowledge is present but the will acts against it. In the minimisation mode (1Ki 16:31), it *distorts* cognition by producing a false moral evaluation that enables continued refusal. Over time, sustained refusal may impair cognition by entrenching the false evaluation (minimisation) as the habitual cognitive framework, making accurate moral assessment progressively harder.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Habitual defection *impairs* the cognitive faculty in the domain of God-knowledge: 1Jo 3:6 — "never... known him" — the knowing of God that the cognitive faculty would normally enable is absent in the habitual sinner. 1Sa 2:25's non-listening impairs the cognitive-receptive function: the corrective input that should update the cognitive framework is refused. The cognitive impairment in habitual defection is thus in the knowledge-of-God dimension specifically: the general cognitive faculty may remain functional, but its capacity for the relational-theological knowing that would correct the sinning pattern is impaired.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Contagious sin *corrupts* the cognitive faculty by feeding it sinning content through authoritative channels. Deu 20:18 — the teaching of abominable practices — corrupts the community's moral-cognitive framework: what they come to know (their cognitive content about worship and conduct) is the Canaanite sinning practices. The corruption is systematic: once the cognitive framework has been reshaped by the transmitted sinning content, the community's moral reasoning operates within a corrupted reference system, making further sinning the natural cognitive output.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(S)*

**[CHAR-8]** S — The corpus does not evidence how political revolt affects the cognitive faculty of the revolting party. The focus is on the political facts (the revolts occurred, the revolts are permanent) rather than the cognitive processes of those involved.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Sinful speech *impairs and distorts* cognition. 1Ti 6:4's "understands nothing" — the person who practices slander from conceit has a cognitive faculty that cannot accurately process reality; the speech is both evidence of the impairment and an agent of its deepening. The beast's constitutive blasphemy (VCG-03) represents the extreme: the creature whose cognition is entirely oriented against God — not merely distorted but wholly captured by anti-God orientation.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — Different patterns per mechanism. (1) *Enticement bypasses cognition*: Jam 1:14 — enticement operates through desire ("his own desire") which precedes deliberate cognitive engagement — the luring occurs before the will has made a reasoned choice. (2) *Hypocrisy distorts cognition*: Gal 2:13 — acting hypocritically alongside others requires cognitive assessment of the social situation ("what will others think?") but produces a distorted moral output; (3) *Apostasy operates through cognitive-instructional distortion*: Act 21:21 — teaching that allegedly redirects the Jews' cognitive understanding of their covenantal obligations.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The condition *distorts* cognition while simultaneously being what the law enables cognition to recognise. (1) *Distortion*: Heb 3:13 — "deceitfulness of sin" — the condition operates through cognitive deception, producing false self-assessment; (2) *Activation by law*: Rom 7:8/9 — "sin, seizing an opportunity through the commandment, produced in me all kinds of covetousness" — the law's cognitive content is seized by the condition to *activate* the very desire the law prohibits; the cognitive-law framework becomes the mechanism of the condition's arousal. The condition thus both evades correct cognition (deception) and exploits the cognitive framework (law) as the occasion for its full expression.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(S)*

**[CHAR-12]** S — No direct evidence. The enslaving power's relationship to cognition is not addressed in the 9-verse corpus.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The record *impairs accurate self-cognition* while being fully known to God.
