Job 13:23 — Job cannot know the extent of his own record without divine disclosure; the cognitive faculty's self-assessment is inherently limited. Jer 16:10 — the people's cognitive question ("What is our iniquity?") under judgment shows the record has accumulated beyond their cognitive self-awareness. Pro 24:9 — conversely, the cognitive act of devising folly creates the record: active scheming is morally registered even if the schemer does not cognise it as sin.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The characteristic *reorients* cognition (Act 2:38 — metanoeō is a cognitive turning) and *is enabled by* cognitive testimony (Act 10:43 — the prophets' witness). The cognitive faculty is not merely passive but actively reoriented: the repentant person's mind has changed direction toward God. The forgiveness received does not bypass cognition (it is not a mystical bypassing of the rational faculty) but operates through it (the prophetic testimony → belief → forgiveness — Act 10:43).

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The generational pattern *impairs* the cognitive integration of divine evidence into faith. Psa 78:32 — "despite his wonders, they did not believe" — the cognitive faculty received the evidence (wonders witnessed) but failed to produce the correct output (belief). The generational unbelief pattern short-circuits the cognitive pathway from evidence to trust: the inherited disposition toward not-believing overrides the cognitive faculty's normal evidence-processing function.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The sinner-identity *distorts* cognition at the self-assessment level. Amo 9:10 — "who say, 'Disaster shall not overtake or meet us'" — the habitual sinner's cognitive self-assessment is wrong; the sinner-identity produces a false cognitive conclusion about the self's safety. Joh 9:24 — "we know that this man is a sinner" — the authoritative religious establishment's cognitive verdict is wrong; the sinner-identity-label is misapplied with cognitive certainty. Conversely, Rom 7:13 — the commandment deepens cognitive understanding of sin's true nature: the cognitive faculty is sharpened by the law's disclosure of what is "sinful beyond measure."

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The guilt-state *clarifies retrospective moral cognition* and can *distort prospective cognition*. Gen 42:21 — the brothers' current guilt-distress clarifies their cognitive understanding of their past act's moral significance. Conversely: Eze 21:23 — "to them it will seem like a false divination" — the guilt-bearing community misinterprets divine judgment as false prophecy; the accumulated guilt distorts the cognitive capacity to correctly interpret what God is doing. Guilt thus has a bi-directional cognitive effect: retrospective clarity, prospective distortion.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Iniquity *distorts* cognition at the self-assessment level and *clarifies* it when conscience operates correctly. Psa 36:2 — the flattering self-cognitive distortion: the mind generates false conclusions about the self's moral condition. Hos 12:8 — the cognitively confident "I am rich... no iniquity in me" is the distorted verdict. Conversely: 2Sa 24:10 — David's cognitive recognition "I have sinned greatly" is accurate; the conscience's correct functioning produces clear cognitive self-assessment. The iniquity distorts cognition in proportion to the suppression of conscience.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Transgression *bypasses and suppresses* cognition in the sustained transgressor. Isa 46:8 — transgressors must be commanded to "recall it to mind" — their cognition has ceased to retain what they know. Pro 28:24 — "that is no transgression" — the deliberate cognitive reframing that suppresses accurate moral knowledge. Jer 2:29 — the people "contend" with God despite having all transgressed — their cognition has inverted, producing self-justifying reasoning instead of accurate self-assessment. Conversely, the cognitive faculty is deployed accurately by the penitent: Psa 51:3 — "I know my transgressions" — precise cognitive self-assessment.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Faithlessness *distorts* cognition at the self-assessment level. 2Ch 28:22 — Ahaz becoming "yet more faithless" under distress — the cognition fails to identify faithlessness as the cause of suffering and therefore misreads the corrective signal; Jer 12:6 — "though they speak friendly words to you" — the treacherous family's cognition generates a false presentation of loyalty while planning betrayal, showing treachery's cognitive deception-mode. The double cognitive movement: the faithless person's cognition produces false appearances of loyalty (Jer 12:6) while blinding itself to its own faithlessness (2Ch 28:22).

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Perversion *corrupts* the cognitive faculty at its root. 1Ti 6:5 — "depraved in mind and deprived of the truth" — the two go together: mind-depravity produces truth-deprivation; the cognitive faculty cannot access truth when it is depraved. Isa 29:16 — the cognitive inversion is so complete the creature can say of its maker "He did not make me" — the most basic cognitive relationship (creature knowing its creator) is perverted. 2Cor 11:3 — the serpent-deceived leading astray of thoughts parallels Eve's deception — the cognitive faculty is corrupted by a deceptive external agent operating on it from outside.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Injustice *misdirects and impairs* cognition. Psa 64:6's "diligent search" for injustice shows cognition misdirected — the analytical capacity deployed toward planning wrong rather than right. Psa 53:1's heart-atheism shows cognition impaired at the foundational level: the mind that denies God cannot accurately perceive the moral order; Job 18:21 — the unjust person "knows not God" — the cognitive God-knowledge deficit produces the inner condition that enables injustice.

---

### T3.2.3

**Question:** What does the pattern of engagement or non-engagement with cognition reveal about the nature of this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The pattern reveals a fundamental tension: wilful sinning is constitutively known (the "wilful" qualifier) yet the will commits it anyway — making the relationship between cognition and volition in sinning one of *knowing-and-nonetheless*. Joseph (Gen 39:9) shows the knowing producing refusal; Saul (1Sa 15:24) shows the knowing overridden by fear. Achan (Jos 7:20) shows the knowing fully present yet insufficient to prevent. This knowing-and-nonetheless dynamic is what distinguishes wilful sinning from CHAR-2 (Unintentional sinning) — the cognitive element is present in CHAR-1 precisely because the will acts against available knowledge.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The cognitive pattern confirms that CHAR-2 is an *information-deficit phenomenon*: the characteristic exists because of the creature's epistemic limitation, not because of the will's defection. Where CHAR-1 shows the cognitive faculty present but overridden (knowing and sinning anyway), CHAR-2 shows the cognitive faculty present but uninformed (not knowing and sinning inadvertently). This distinction is the analytical key: the cognitive faculty's state — informed vs uninformed — is what separates CHAR-1 from CHAR-2, not the moral weight of the act or the objective liability it creates.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that the *precision of confession reflects the depth of cognitive engagement with sin*. Exo 9:27 (Pharaoh — brief, unprecise) represents minimal cognitive engagement; Dan 9:5–15 (extended, multi-dimensional) represents maximal. The characteristic invites and rewards cognitive deepening — the person who confesses more specifically is engaging their mind more fully with the moral reality of what they have done. This confirms that confession is not merely an emotional or volitional act but a cognitive-moral event.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that conscience suppression is an *epistemic moral failure*: it is what happens when the mind's knowledge of God (1Cor 15:34) and the mind's self-directed moral reasoning (Jer 16:10) both fail. The cognitive failure is not general unintelligence but a specific blindness in the self-moral-assessment domain. This is why Paul's cure for the cognitive failure is not clever argument but "knowledge of God" (1Cor 15:34) — the cognitive faculty needs its fundamental reference point (God) restored before accurate moral self-cognition becomes possible.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals a critical distinction within CHAR-5: the Heb 10:26 mode shows the will acting against correct knowledge (knowledge present, will bypasses it), while the 1Ki 16:31 mode shows the will enabled by distorted knowledge (knowledge corrupted by minimisation). In both cases the will refuses, but the cognitive configuration differs: in one, the problem is purely volitional (know but don't turn); in the other, the problem combines volitional and cognitive failure (don't turn partly because know wrongly).

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals a critical distinction between general cognition (which may be intact) and God-knowledge (which is impaired). 1Jo 3:6's "never known him" is not a general intelligence statement but a theological-relational one: the habitual sinner may be intelligent and capable, but their cognitive access to the knowledge of Christ is blocked by or correlates with the habitual sinning pattern. This reveals that correction of habitual defection requires specifically the restoration of God-knowledge, not merely cognitive information about right behaviour. 1Jo 3:6's abiding relationship is the knowledge-condition that breaks the pattern.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that contagious sin is partly a cognitive phenomenon: the community's knowledge base about right worship and conduct is being shaped by the transmitted sinning content (Deu 20:18 — taught abominable practices). The cognitive corruption is why structural dismantling (2Ki 23:15 — demolishing the altar) must accompany inner moral renewal: if the altar remains, the community's cognitive framework continues to receive sinning content from its presence; both the structural and cognitive sources of the transmitted sinning must be addressed.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that the corpus is not primarily concerned with the inner cognitive processes of political revolt but with its political-historical outcomes. The cognitive engagement implied (political calculation, strategic assessment) is the background condition for revolt, not the corpus's explicit focus. The corpus's literary form (regnal narrative and historical summary) describes political facts rather than cognitive processes.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that sinful speech operates within and deepens cognitive distortion: accurate cognition of God's identity and moral reality would prevent sinful speech (1Ti 1:13 — the ignorance was the enabling condition; receiving knowledge/mercy reversed it). The corrective for sinful speech is therefore not merely behavioural but cognitive-theological: the person must come to know what they do not currently know. This is why 1Ti 1:13's reversal by mercy is possible — the ignorance-ground of blasphemy is addressable by the knowledge of God that mercy brings.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that the three mechanisms engage cognition differently: enticement primarily bypasses it (desire precedes deliberation — Jam 1:14), hypocrisy distorts it (social cognition produces wrong moral output — Gal 2:13), and apostasy targets it through instruction (Act 21:21 — teaching reshapes what is believed about covenantal identity). This diversity confirms the characteristic's composite nature: the mechanisms operate at different cognitive levels and through different cognitive engagements.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that the condition's relationship to cognition is complex and bidirectional: (1) the law as a cognitive framework reveals the condition (Rom 3:20 — knowledge of sin comes through law); (2) but the condition exploits the same cognitive framework for its own activation (Rom 7:8 — sin seizes the commandment). This is why Paul argues in Romans 7 that the law is not sin (Rom 7:7) — the law is good; the condition is what uses the good cognitive framework perversely. The cognitive faculty's recognition of the condition (through the law) does not by itself produce liberation from the condition (Rom 7:23 — the mind's law is overridden by the law of sin in the members).

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(S)*

**[CHAR-12]** S — The silence on the cognitive faculty is consistent with the corpus's focus on the will's captivity (not a cognitive phenomenon) and the identity-allegiance level (1Jo 3:8 — of the devil — a constitutional identity rather than a cognitive assessment). The enslaving power operates below or beside the cognitive level. Noted.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals an asymmetry between divine and human cognition of the record: God knows it fully (Amo 5:12 — "for I know how many are your transgressions and how great are your sins") while the human's cognitive access to their own record is partial and often distorted. This asymmetry is the characteristic's epistemic structure: the record is fully known to God and partially (or wrongly) known to the person. Isa 58:1 — the prophetic function is precisely to bridge this asymmetry: "declare to my people their transgression, to the house of Jacob their sins."

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that the characteristic is not anti-rational: the seeking requires cognitive recognition of sin (Lev 4:14), cognitive engagement with the prophetic testimony (Act 10:43), and cognitive reorientation through repentance (Act 2:38). But cognition alone does not complete the characteristic: Exo 32:30 — Moses knowing the sin and going up to seek atonement does not guarantee forgiveness; he says "perhaps I can make atonement" — the cognitive-volitional seeking is necessary but the divine response is not mechanical.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that generational sin impairs not raw cognitive capacity (the people perceived the wonders) but *cognitive-moral integration*: the capacity to interpret what is cognitively registered in light of the correct moral-theological framework. Psa 78:32's unbelief despite wonders is a failure of moral-theological cognition rather than sensory cognition. The generational pattern corrupts the interpretive framework through which evidence is processed rather than eliminating the capacity to perceive evidence.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals an asymmetry in the sinner-identity's cognitive effects: applied to the self by the honest conscience, the sinner-label is accurate (Luk 5:8; Luk 18:13; 1Ti 1:15 — Paul's "foremost sinner"); applied to others by the self-righteous, it is a tool of false categorisation (Joh 9:24 — Jesus wrongly labelled). The cognitive faculty is most accurate when the sinner recognises their own condition (self-directed cognition) and least accurate when it is deployed to identify and exclude others (other-directed cognition from a position of self-righteousness).

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that guilt-as-inner-state is not exclusively a feeling but has a specific cognitive structure: it requires knowledge to activate (Lev 5:2/3/4 — "when he comes to know"), generates retrospective causal reasoning (Gen 42:21 — connecting past guilt to present suffering), and can distort prospective interpretation (Eze 9:9; Eze 21:23). The cognitive faculty is the gateway to guilt-recognition and is itself shaped (both clarified and distorted) by the guilt it mediates.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that iniquity-as-accumulated-crime is not cognitively self-disclosing: the person does not automatically know their iniquity-accumulation through unaided reason. Gen 15:16 — the Amorites are presumably unaware their iniquity is being measured toward completion; Jer 16:10 — "what is our iniquity? What is the sin that we have committed?" — the cognitively unaware community cannot identify its own crime without prophetic disclosure. The cognitive faculty requires external input (divine revelation, prophetic word, conscience-strike) to accurately assess the iniquity-account.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals the most distinctive analytical finding for CHAR-19: transgression requires cognition (Rom 4:15 — law must be known) yet progressively suppresses the cognitive capacity to accurately assess the transgression being committed (Isa 46:8; Pro 28:24). The knowing that makes transgression possible is the same knowing that transgression systematically undermines. The paradox of CHAR-19's cognitive dimension: it requires knowledge to begin and produces ignorance to sustain itself.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that faithlessness is particularly damaging to the cognitive faculty's self-assessment function: the faithless person cannot accurately read the signal that distress sends (2Ch 28:22) and cannot accurately present themselves to others (Jer 12:6 — friendly words masking treachery). The cognitive function is co-opted: used to plan treachery (Psa 59:5) rather than to assess covenant loyalty honestly. The corrective is Ezr 10:2 — "we have broken faith with our God" — the honest cognitive confession that accurately names what the person has done.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals that perversion produces a two-stage cognitive corruption: (1) primary cognitive faculty impairment (1Ti 6:5 — mind depraved) and (2) consequent truth-deprivation (1Ti 6:5 — "deprived of the truth"). The depraved mind cannot self-correct because the very faculty of self-assessment is what has been corrupted. Isa 29:16's creator-denial confirms: perversion corrupts cognition at the most fundamental level — the capacity to know the source of one's existence. The cognitive pattern makes CHAR-21 the most epistemically devastating characteristic in M10.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The cognitive pattern reveals two levels: (1) *Surface-level misdirection* — the mind is fully deployed but toward wrong ends (Psa 64:6 — diligent search for injustice); (2) *Foundation-level deficit* — the God-knowledge that would anchor just cognition is absent (Job 18:21). The most analytically significant finding: Psa 64:6 — "for the inward mind and heart of a man are deep" — Scripture itself acknowledges the cognitive depth from which injustice's devising emerges; the deep mind is the site of injustice's planning.

---

## T3.3 — Memory

### T3.3.1

**Question:** Does this characteristic engage the memory faculty — the holding and retrieving of inner-being reality across time — and if so, how?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The memory faculty is engaged in two directions. (1) *Pre-act memory as deterrent*: Psa 119:11 ("I have stored up your word in my heart") — the word memorised is what prevents sinning; the memory faculty holds the moral resource that deters the will. (2) *Post-act memory as reckoning*: Gen 42:22 — Reuben's retrieval of his past warning ("Did I not tell you not to sin against the boy?") in the present moment of distress shows memory connecting the past act of sinning to present consequence. The cultic-atonement corpus (VCG-04: Lev 4:23 — "the sin which he has committed is made known to him") shows memory-retrieval as the trigger for cultic obligation when the sin surfaces to conscious recollection.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Memory is engaged in two directions. (1) *Pre-act memory failure*: unintentional sinning can occur when a known prohibition fails to be recalled at the moment of action — the person may have been taught the commandment but did not retrieve it in the moment (Lev 4:2 — the commandments "about things not to be done" presupposes prior instruction; the sinning occurs despite that instruction, implying a memory-retrieval gap). (2) *Post-act memory activation*: the realisation phase (Lev 5:5 — "when he realizes his guilt") involves the person connecting the past act to its moral status — a retrospective memory-event where the act is re-evaluated in light of newly available information.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Yes. Memory is engaged in confession's retrospective orientation — confession names what was *done*, requiring retrieval of the past act. Neh 1:6 — "confessing the sins... which we have sinned against you. Even I and my father's house have sinned" — requires memory spanning generations. Psa 106:6 — "Both we and our fathers have sinned" — memory of ancestral sin extended to the present generation. Dan 9:11 — the curse written in Moses's Law "have been poured out upon us, because we have sinned against him" — historical memory of the Mosaic covenant's terms. Confession thus requires an active memory faculty that retrieves both the specific past acts and the longer moral history they belong to.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Memory is implicitly engaged through the *failure to hold the moral record*. Jer 16:10 — "what sin have we committed against the Lord our God?" — the people cannot retrieve (or perhaps never encoded) the moral record of their accumulated sinning. The memory faculty that should hold the history of what was done is either not functioning or not accessing the relevant record. Jer 2:35 — "I have not sinned" — the active denial may involve suppression of the memory of specific sins: the person cannot (or will not) retrieve what their conscience should hold before them.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Memory is implicitly engaged through the inherited-pattern dimension. 2Ki 15:9 — "as his fathers had done" — the son who follows his fathers in not departing from Jeroboam's sins carries the memory of his fathers' pattern as the inherited moral framework. The memory of "this is how things have been done" provides the cognitive-memorial basis for the inherited refusal. Neh 9:29 — the corporate prayer recites the history of Israel's stubborn refusal over generations — the memory faculty holds this history as the community's self-understanding.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Memory is implicitly engaged through the historical-persistence evidence. Hos 10:9 — "from the days of Gibeah, you have sinned, O Israel; there they have *continued*" — the sinning has persisted across a history that extends from Gibeah to the present; the habitual pattern is maintained across time, which requires that the sinning orientation be preserved in the community's moral memory and practice. 2Ki 17:7 — "the people of Israel had sinned against the Lord their God, who had brought them up out of Egypt" — the forgetting of the redemptive history (the Exodus) is the memory-failure that enables the habitual sinning: the memory of what God did is not operational as a corrective in the will's direction.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Memory is engaged through the historical-generational persistence of the Jeroboam pattern. 1Ki 22:52 — Ahaziah followed "the way of his father and... his mother and... Jeroboam the son of Nebat, who made Israel to sin" — the memory of the transmitted sinning pattern is held across three named generations and referenced as the operative moral precedent. The memory faculty carries the Jeroboam-pattern as the received moral heritage; each subsequent generation's reception of the pattern requires the memory of what prior generations did.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — Memory is engaged through the "to this day" markers. 1Ki 12:19 / 2Ch 10:19 — "Israel has been in rebellion against the house of David to this day" — the narrator's memory holds the revolt as a persistent present reality; the community's corporate memory carries the fact of the revolt and its ongoing consequence. 2Ki 8:22 / 2Ch 21:10 — "Edom revolted from the rule of Judah to this day" — similarly. The memory faculty holds the revolt in the historical-political consciousness, making the broken relationship a known and named part of the political identity.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(S)*

**[CHAR-9]** S — The 22-verse corpus does not explicitly engage the memory faculty in connection with sinful speech. No verse addresses the retention or retrieval of past sinful speech or the role of memory in enabling or preventing it.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(S)*

**[CHAR-10]** S — The 5-verse corpus does not explicitly engage the memory faculty in connection with any of the three mechanisms. The characteristic's focus is on present-tense processes (being enticed, acting hypocritically, forsaking) rather than on memory retrieval or retention.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Memory is engaged through the ledger/accounting dimension of the condition. Rom 4:8 — "blessed is the man against whom the Lord will not count his sin" — sin is "counted" against the person; the divine memory of the person's sin-condition constitutes the ledger. Act 3:19 — "that your sins may be blotted out" — blotting out is a memory-erasure metaphor: the divine record is cleared. 1Ti 5:24 — "the sins of some people are conspicuous, going before them to judgment, but the sins of others appear later" — the temporal dimension of when the condition's recorded content appears implies a memory-record that either surfaces now or at judgment.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(S)*

**[CHAR-12]** S — The 9-verse corpus does not explicitly engage the memory faculty in connection with sin as enslaving power. The characteristic's focus on present-tense practice, structural imprisonment, and constitutional identity does not engage memory processes.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The memory faculty is the primary faculty engaged — on both the divine and human sides. (1) *Divine memory*: Jer 14:10 — "he will remember their iniquity and punish their sins"; Hos 8:13 — "now he will remember their iniquity and punish their sins" — the divine memory retains the record and triggers judgment from it. The liberation metaphors are memory-erasure metaphors: Isa 43:25 — "I will not remember your sins"; Jer 31:34 — "I will remember their sin no more." (2) *Human memory*: Gen 41:9 — "I remember my offenses today" — the cupbearer's memory of past failure resurfaces; Psa 25:7 — "Remember not the sins of my youth" — the psalmist pleads for God not to hold the past record in memory against him.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Yes — through the Day of Atonement's memorial function. Exo 30:16 — "that it may bring the people of Israel to remembrance before the Lord, so as to make atonement for your lives" — the atonement money brings Israel to God's remembrance; the memorial function is part of the seeking mechanism. Gen 41:9 (CHAR-13 corpus — same root) — "I remember my offenses today" — the memory of past failure that triggers the seeking. In the characteristic itself: the annual kip.pu.rim cycle (VCG-01) operates as a rhythmic memorial practice — annual remembrance that the sin-record requires covering.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Yes — through the explicit memory-failure dimension. Psa 78:32 — "despite his wonders, they did not believe" — and the broader Psalm 78 context (a historical recital of God's acts that Israel failed to remember — the psalm's genre is anamnesis, calling the community to remember what was forgotten). Isa 64:5 — "those who remember you in your ways" — the positive foil states that *remembering* God's ways is what the generational sinning-pattern fails to do; the inherited condition is characterised by a failure of covenantal memory.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Memory engages through the persistent reputation and the sin-accumulation dimensions. Num 16:38 — "these men who have sinned at the cost of their lives... shall be a sign to the people of Israel" — the sinners' identity is permanently memorialised in the bronze altar covering; their sinner-character is preserved as a communal memory-warning; Luk 7:37 — the woman "of the city, who was a sinner" — the city's corporate memory has fixed her identity as "a sinner"; Psa 51:13 — "sinners will return to you" — the memory of what the sinner was (before return) is the implicit backdrop of the return.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Yes — centrally. Eze 21:23/24 — "he brings their guilt to remembrance... because you have made your guilt to be remembered" — God's recall of guilt from memory is the mechanism of judgment; the guilt-record exists in God's memory; Eze 21:24 — "your transgressions are uncovered, so that in all your deeds your sins appear" — the guilt becomes visible when God's memory re-activates it. Gen 42:21 — the brothers' own memory of their past cruelty activates their guilt-recognition; their guilt is memory-mediated.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Yes — centrally through the divine memory register (VCG-03). Hos 13:12 — "the iniquity of Ephraim is bound up; his sin is kept in store" — God's memory is the storage medium for the iniquity-record; Hos 9:9 — "he will remember their iniquity; he will punish their sins" — divine memory is the mechanism that activates judgment; Psa 79:8 — "do not remember against us our former iniquities" — the plea asks God's memory not to function against them. Human memory also engages: Lev 26:40/41 — "confess their iniquity and the iniquity of their fathers" — the generational confession requires memory of ancestral crimes.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Yes — through both divine memory and human memory. (1) *Divine memory of transgression*: Isa 43:25 — "I will not remember your sins" — forgiveness is defined as divine memory-non-retention; the default is that God remembers; Dan 9:24 — "to finish the transgression" — a temporally bounded divine memory; (2) *Human memory of transgression*: Isa 46:8 — "recall it to mind, you transgressors" — human memory of the transgressing condition is required; Psa 25:7 — "remember not the sins of my youth or my transgressions" — the plea for God not to remember the speaker's early transgressions.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Yes — through the communal memory and the eschatological forgetting dimensions. (1) *Communal warning-memory*: 2Ch 30:7 — "do not be like your fathers and your brothers, who were faithless to the Lord God of their fathers, so that he made them a desolation, as you see" — the appeal to visible historical memory of what faithlessness produced; (2) *Eschatological resolution of memory*: Eze 39:26 — "they shall forget their shame and all the treachery they have practiced against me, when they dwell securely" — the eschatological promise is that the memory of treachery will be eclipsed by secure dwelling; (3) *Divine covenant-memory triggered by confession*: Lev 26:42 — "I will remember my covenant with Jacob" — God's covenant-memory is reactivated by the confession of treachery.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The memory faculty is engaged through the forgetting-God pattern. Jer 3:21 — "they have perverted their way; they have forgotten the Lord their God" — the perversion of the way is explicitly linked to forgetting God; memory of God is what the perversion eliminates. Pro 2:17 (cross-context) — forgetting covenant; Deu 32:20 — the perverse generation — "children in whom is no faithfulness" — the absence of faithfulness implies the forgetting of the covenant-bond that faithfulness maintained.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Yes — through divine memory and communal warning-memory. (1) *Divine memory*: Rev 18:5 — "God has remembered her iniquities" — God's memory retains the accumulating record of injustice; (2) *Memory of God's justice*: Psa 107:42 — "the upright see it and are glad, and all wickedness shuts its mouth" — the righteous remember God's just acts which silence wickedness; (3) *Communal warning-memory*: 2Sa 7:10 — "violent men shall afflict them no more, as formerly" — the memory of past injustice informs the promise.

---

### T3.3.2

**Question:** Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair memory in the person?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Wilful sinning can *impair* retrieval of the morally relevant memory at the critical moment. The mechanism: sinning proceeds when stored moral knowledge is not retrieved effectively by the memory faculty in the moment of decision. The prescriptions in VCG-03 (Psa 119:11 — store the word; Exo 20:20 — keep fear before you) function as memory-maintenance measures: ensuring the relevant moral content is accessible at the moment of temptation. Where memory retrieval fails — where the word is not "stored" or the fear is not "before" the person — the will is less equipped to resist sinning.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Unintentional sinning involves memory *bypassed* in its retrieval function at the critical moment: the stored knowledge (of the commandment) was not retrieved when the relevant act was being performed. The remedial system (Lev 4:2; Num 15:27/28) is designed to address this recurring retrieval failure by providing a standing remedy rather than relying solely on the person's consistent recall. The Levitical instruction corpus (implied by Lev 4:2's reference to "the Lord's commandments") is itself a memory-formation system — teaching the commandments so they are available for retrieval.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Confession *activates and deepens* the memory faculty by requiring its active deployment. The act of naming what was done (2Sa 24:10 — "what I have done"; 1Ch 21:17 — "I who gave command to number the people") involves retrieving the specific act from memory and holding it before God. Extended confession (Dan 9:5; Neh 1:6) activates corporate and historical memory — the confessor draws on the full moral history of their community to name the pattern within which the specific sin belongs. This extended deployment of memory is itself a formative practice.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Conscience suppression *impairs* the morally relevant memory function — specifically the retrieval of one's own moral record. Jer 16:10 — the community cannot access the memory of its sinning. The impairment is selective: the people presumably remember other things, but the moral record of their sinning before God is not accessible to them. This selective memory impairment is what makes Jer 16:10's question sincere rather than disingenuous.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Refusal to repent *selectively activates* the memory of inherited patterns (the fathers' example — 2Ki 15:9) while potentially suppressing or not accessing the memory of what the sinful course has cost. The person who "did not depart from the sins of Jeroboam" holds the memory of that pattern as the natural path while not engaging the memory of the consequences (loss of standing, Eze 18:24; foreclosure of remedy, Heb 10:26). Memory is engaged in the reinforcement direction (reinforcing the inherited pattern) but not in the corrective direction (recalling the costs of the sinful course).

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Habitual defection *selectively impairs* memory in the direction of moral self-correction: 2Ki 17:7 — "the Lord their God, who had brought them up out of the land of Egypt" — the failure to hold the memory of the Exodus as an operative corrective in their inner-being orientation enables the sustained defection. The memory of redemption (which should produce gratitude, allegiance, and continued covenant faithfulness) is not functioning to correct the sinning direction. The habitual pattern maintains itself partly by not holding the corrective memory as active and motivating.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Contagious sin *shapes* the memory faculty by establishing the sinning pattern as the memorable precedent that shapes subsequent moral memory. The Jeroboam-formula's repetition across 12+ verses shows this: the memory of Jeroboam's contagious sin becomes the generational reference point against which every subsequent king is measured. The memory faculty is operating — it holds and transmits the Jeroboam pattern — but what it holds and transmits is the sinning precedent rather than the covenant faithfulness precedent. Memory is engaged in the service of the sinning tradition.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — Political revolt *establishes* a persistent corporate memory of the broken relationship. The "to this day" markers show that the revolt has created a permanent fixture in the community's political memory: the break is remembered and named as an ongoing reality. This is memory deepened in the negative direction — the revolt is so significant that it becomes a permanent reference point in the community's political-historical self-understanding.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(S)*

**[CHAR-9]** S — No evidence. The corpus is silent on the memory faculty's engagement with sinful speech.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(S)*

**[CHAR-10]** S — No evidence. The corpus is silent on the memory faculty's engagement with any of the three mechanisms.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The condition *creates a divine memory record* (the ledger) and shapes human memory through the burden it generates. 2Ti 3:6 — "burdened with sins" — the accumulated condition weighs on the person's inner carrying-capacity; the burden involves the memory of accumulated sins that weigh the person down. Act 7:60 — Stephen's dying prayer: "do not hold this sin against them" — the prayer presupposes that God's memory will hold the act against the perpetrators unless released; sin's ledger-dimension is a divine memorial record.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(S)*

**[CHAR-12]** S — No evidence. The corpus is silent on the memory faculty's engagement with sin's enslaving power.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The divine-record characteristic *is constituted by divine memory*: the record exists as God's memorial of every sin committed. It deepens divine memory's function (every sin is retained — Hos 13:12 — "kept in store") while the characteristic's resolution is precisely divine memory-erasure. For human memory: the record can deepen human memory of past failures (Psa 51:3 — "my sin is ever before me"; Gen 41:9 — "I remember my offenses today") or be suppressed through wilful forgetting (Jer 16:10 — people cannot identify their sin). The characteristic thus operates through, and in some cases against, the human memory faculty.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Forgiveness received *transforms* the memory relationship to sin. Jer 31:34 (CHAR-13 corpus) — "I will remember their sin no more" — the received forgiveness changes the divine memory's relationship to the sin-record. For the human person: the forgiveness received does not erase the memory of what was forgiven (Luk 7:47 — the woman remembers her many sins as the backdrop of her love-response) but transforms the memory's charge: the remembered sin is now remembered as forgiven, not as outstanding.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The generational sinning pattern *impairs* covenantal memory. Psa 78:32 — "despite his wonders, they did not believe" — the failure to remember God's acts in a way that produces present trust is the memory-impairment. Isa 64:5 — "those who remember you in your ways" (the positive opposite of the generational sinners) — the generational sin-condition is characterised by the failure to remember God's ways in a life-shaping manner. The community forgets what it witnessed, and the forgetting enables the sinning to continue.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The sinner-identity *deepens* the community's negative memorial of the sinner. Num 16:38 — the bronze altar covering functions as a permanent memorial of the sinners' identity and fate; the sin is archived in the altar structure permanently. Luk 7:37/39 — "a woman of the city, who was a sinner" — the community's memory of her sinner-identity is fixed and recalled instantly when she appears; the memory persists. Conversely, Luk 15:7/10 — the sinner who repents generates a new memorial (joy in heaven) that overrides the prior sinner-memorial with the repentance-memorial.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Guilt *deepens morally significant memory*. Gen 42:21 — under the pressure of current distress, the brothers' memory of their past cruelty becomes vivid and morally interpretive; the guilt-state makes the past memorable in its moral dimensions. Conversely, the attempt to escape guilt through self-cleansing (Jer 2:22 — "wash yourself with lye") is an attempt to bypass the moral memory that guilt maintains; but the guilt "is still before me" — God's memory of the guilt persists regardless of the person's self-cleansing effort.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Iniquity *deepens* divine memory (the crime persists in God's record — Hos 13:12 — sealed) while *impairing* human memory of the iniquity's significance. Jer 16:10 — the community's question "what is our iniquity?" shows that the human memory of the community's own accumulated crime has faded; they cannot remember what they have done. The prophetic tradition's task is to restore the community's memory of its iniquity: Lam 2:14 — "they have not exposed your iniquity to restore your fortunes" — the false prophets failed to bring the iniquity back to communal memory. God's memory of the iniquity and human memory of it are thus inversely proportional in many cases.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Transgression *impairs* the person's self-directed memory (Isa 46:8 — must be commanded to recall) while being *deepened* in God's memory (Isa 43:25 — only God can choose not to remember). The person who transgresses progressively loses the memory of their transgression's significance (Isa 46:8 — "recall it to mind"); God's memory of the transgression grows more rather than less acute (Amos 5:12 — "I know how many"). Forgiveness inverts this: God actively chooses not to remember (Isa 43:25) while the forgiven person's memory of their transgression becomes the basis of teaching others (Psa 51:13 — "I will teach transgressors your ways").

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Faithlessness *suppresses* the corrective memory that would prevent further betrayal (2Ch 30:7 — the fathers' desolation should be remembered as a warning). Ezr 9:4 — the community's faithlessness was not remembered until Ezra confronted it. The corrective act of confession (Lev 26:40 — "if they confess their iniquity and... treachery") is simultaneously a memory-act — recalling what was done against God. The eschatological resolution (Eze 39:26) involves a paradoxical forgetting of the treachery — not suppression but the eclipsing of shame by restored relationship.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Perversion *suppresses* the spiritually significant memory. Jer 3:21 — "perverted their way... forgotten the Lord their God" — the perversion of the life-direction produces the forgetting of God; the causal link is direct. The forgetting is not neutral loss but the active suppression of the memory that would recall the covenant-bond. This connects CHAR-21 to CHAR-20: the faithlessness that CHAR-20 describes is enabled by CHAR-21's perversion-induced forgetting of the covenant-partner (God).

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Injustice *suppresses* the memory of God's justice that would check wrong conduct. Psa 53:1 — "there is no God" in the heart — the denial of God is simultaneously a suppression of the memory of God's moral authority; Hos 10:13 — "trusted in your own way" — self-reliance suppresses the memory of God's justice. The restorative act of God displaying his justice (Psa 107:42 — "the upright see it and are glad") reactivates the memory that silences wickedness.

---

### T3.3.3

**Question:** What does the pattern of engagement or non-engagement with memory reveal about the nature of this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The pattern reveals that wilful sinning involves a *memory-defeat*: the knowledge is present in principle, but the memory faculty fails to make it operative at the decisive moment. This is confirmed by the prescriptive counter-measures (store the word, keep fear before you) — they function as memory-activation strategies. The characteristic is thus in part a failure of the memory faculty to function as a moral resource in the moment — not a failure of knowledge but of recall-in-the-moment.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The pattern reveals that unintentional sinning exploits the *gap between stored knowledge and active retrieval*. A person may know a commandment in principle (stored) but fail to retrieve it at the moment an action is being taken. The Levitical system does not resolve this gap entirely (hence the standing remedial institution) but provides a pathway when the gap results in sin. This is a realistic engagement with human creaturely limitation: storing knowledge does not guarantee its retrieval in every situation, and the prescribed remedy addresses the moral liability that arises from retrieval failures.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The memory pattern reveals that confession is a *memory-activating event*: it requires and exercises the capacity to hold the past honestly — not to forget, minimise, or reframe what was done. The corporate confessions (Psa 106:6; Jer 3:25; Lam 5:7 — "our fathers sinned") show that confession can extend memory across generations, naming the multigenerational moral reality honestly. This honest memory is what genuine confession produces and what hollow confession fails to sustain — hollow confession does not hold the past long enough to let it shape the will's future direction.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The memory pattern reveals that conscience suppression disrupts the *continuity of moral self-knowledge across time*. Accurate moral self-perception requires remembering what was done — the Jer 16:10 people lack this continuity. This is why the prophetic word functions as a memory supply: the prophet reminds the people of their history (Jer 16:11–13 context — "your fathers forsook me...") because their own moral memory has failed. The characteristic thus reveals that conscience suppression is not only a present-moment failure but a *temporal moral amnesia*.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The memory pattern reveals that refusal to repent is sustained partly by *selective memory*: the memory of the inherited sinful pattern is operative (providing precedent and norm), while the memory of its moral gravity and consequences is not operative. Neh 9:29's communal prayer recites the history precisely to break this selective memory — by naming what the community has repeatedly done despite warnings, the prayer aims to make the suppressed moral-memory accessible. The corrective of refusal to repent requires making the memory of the sinful course's costs as vivid as the memory of the course itself.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The memory pattern reveals that habitual defection is sustained in part through *failure of redemptive memory*: the community that has forgotten (or does not hold operationally) the memory of what God did in bringing them out of Egypt (2Ki 17:7) has lost the memorial anchor that should orient their loyalty. Habitual defection is thus partly a memory disorder: the sustained sinning orientation has overwritten or suppressed the corrective memory of God's redemptive acts. This is the inverse of liturgical memory (the covenant memorial practices that were designed to maintain the community's orientation): when those memorial practices are replaced by altars for sinning (Hos 8:11), the community's memory is shaped by the sinning pattern rather than by the redemptive history.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The memory pattern reveals that contagious sin is self-perpetuating through memory: the Jeroboam pattern is held in the community's corporate moral memory and retrieved as the operative norm for each subsequent generation's leaders (1Ki 22:52 — followed the way of father, mother, and Jeroboam). This is the memory-based mechanism of generational contagious sin: the specific sinning pattern is remembered, named, and followed, giving it a persistent cognitive-memorial presence that operates independently of the original leader's personal authority. Josiah's demolition of the altar (2Ki 23:15) addresses the structural deposit; but the memory of the Jeroboam pattern itself persists in the historical record.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The memory pattern reveals that political revolt becomes a *defining historical memory*: the revolts of Israel from the Davidic house and of Edom and Libnah from Judah are remembered "to this day" — they have become constitutive of the political-historical identity of the revolting peoples. This is the memory dimension of the political deposit (T2.8.1): the revolt is not merely a physical-structural fracture but a memory-level fracture — the broken relationship is held in corporate political memory as the defining fact of the community's identity in relation to the governing authority.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(S)*

**[CHAR-9]** S — The silence on memory is consistent with sinful speech's present-tense speech-act character: the characteristic is about the utterance as an event, not about memory processes. Noted.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(S)*

**[CHAR-10]** S — The silence on memory is consistent with the mechanisms' orientation toward present-active processes rather than memory-based operations. For apostasy (Act 21:21), forgetting of covenantal identity might be implied by forsaking Moses, but memory is not explicitly named. Noted.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The memory pattern reveals that the universal sin-condition creates a *moral record* — a divine memorial of the condition's expression — that either stands against the person (Rom 4:8 — "counted against") or is released (Act 3:19 — "blotted out"; Rom 4:7 — "covered"). The condition is not merely present-tense subjective experience but past-tense accumulated record. This memorial dimension is why forgiveness (VCG-02) is specifically framed in accounting metaphors: blotting out, covering, not counting — all operations on the record, not merely on the present experience of the condition.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(S)*

**[CHAR-12]** S — The silence on memory reflects the characteristic's focus on the present-state of captivity (slave-to-sin — Joh 8:34) rather than on historical accumulation or memory-retrieval processes. Noted.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The memory pattern reveals that CHAR-13 is uniquely memory-constituted among M10 characteristics: the divine record is divine memory made judicial — every sin is retained in God's memory as a standing moral account. Jer 31:34 — "I will remember their sin no more" — the new-covenant liberation is defined as divine memory-cancellation, not merely divine forgiveness-declaration. The characteristic thus reveals: sin's ultimate hold over the person is not primarily in inner experience or constitutional damage but in divine memory's retention of the moral account — and liberation is the permanent cancellation of that retention.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The memory pattern reveals an asymmetry in how forgiveness transforms memory: the divine memory is erased (Jer 31:34 — remembered no more); the human memory is not erased but recontextualised (Luk 7:47 — the many sins remembered as the measure of grace received). This asymmetry is analytically significant: the characteristic does not produce human amnesia about past sin but transforms the sin-memory's relationship to the self. The Exo 30:16 atonement-money bringing Israel to divine remembrance also shows the memorial function operating in the seeking-direction: the seeker needs to be remembered before God, not forgotten.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The memory pattern reveals that generational sin perpetuates itself partly through *covenantal memory failure*: the community fails to transmit an effective memory of God's acts and character across generations, leaving each new generation without the formative memory that would orient them toward trust. Psa 78's genre (a historical recital of Israel's failures) is itself a corrective to this memory-failure: the psalm attempts to do what the generational pattern prevents — transmit an accurate covenantal memory that enables trust rather than repetition of the sinning-pattern.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The memory pattern reveals that the sinner-identity has remarkable memorial persistence: Num 16:38 — permanently inscribed in the altar bronze; Luk 7:37 — fixed in the city's collective knowledge. The identity sticks in community memory far beyond the individual sinning acts that formed it. The only memorial that overrides it is the repentance-memorial (Luk 15:7 — joy in heaven over one sinner who repents) — but this requires the decisive inner turning that transforms the identity from sinner-as-who-I-am to repentant-sinner-as-who-I-was.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The memory pattern reveals that guilt-as-inner-state is constitutively memory-dependent on two sides: (1) the guilt requires the person's memory of the violating act (or its coming-to-knowledge) to activate; (2) God's memory of the guilt constitutes the divine-record dimension that the guilt-state reflects (Eze 21:23 — God bringing guilt "to remembrance"). CHAR-17's subjective experience of guilt is the human-side experience of what CHAR-13's divine record holds on the God-side. The memory connection between the two characteristics is structural.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The memory pattern reveals that iniquity-as-accumulated-crime operates as a two-memory reality: it is perpetually held in God's memory (Hos 13:12 — "kept in store"; Psa 90:8 — "set before you") while simultaneously fading from human memory (Jer 16:10 — community's question). The contrast is the prophetic task: Jer 14:7 — "our iniquities testify against us" — the prophet restores the community's memory of its own crime by giving the iniquity a voice as a witness. The eschatological resolution addresses both memories: Jer 31:34 — "I will remember their sin no more" — God's memory is the one that must ultimately be resolved for the iniquity to be truly gone.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The memory pattern reveals a double-inversion at forgiveness: God, who has been remembering the transgression (Amos 5:12), chooses to forget (Isa 43:25); the person, who had been forgetting their transgression (Isa 46:8), now correctly remembers it in order to teach from it (Psa 51:13). The memory reversal at forgiveness is constitutive: the forgiven transgressor's accurate memory of past transgression becomes the very resource for guiding other transgressors back (Psa 51:13 — "teach transgressors your ways, and sinners will return to you").

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The memory pattern reveals that faithlessness and memory stand in inverse relationship: faithlessness is enabled when the memory of God's covenant-expectation and the consequences of past faithlessness is suppressed (2Ch 28:22 — Ahaz not remembering); faithlessness is addressed when memory is activated (Lev 26:40 — confession recalls the treachery). Eze 39:26's eschatological forgetting of shame is not the suppression of memory but its transcendence: the faithful-dwelling-secure state has so eclipsed the treacherous-past that the shame no longer defines the community's identity.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The memory pattern reveals that perversion operates by displacing the spiritually anchoring memories (memory of God, of the covenant, of the created order) with the inward orientation toward distorted objects. Jer 3:21 — "perverted their way; forgotten the Lord" — the perversion and the forgetting are simultaneous and causally linked. The restoration from perversion (returning to God — Jer 3:12-13) requires the reactivation of memory (acknowledgment of guilt) alongside the volitional turning.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The memory pattern reveals that injustice is sustained by suppressing the memory of God's justice-standard. The unjust person's cognitive subsystem operates with God's justice-memory blocked: their "diligent search for injustice" (Psa 64:6) replaces what the God-fearing person's memory of divine justice would naturally prevent. The reversal requires reactivating divine-justice memory (2Ch 19:7 — "let the fear of the Lord be upon you... there is no injustice with the Lord our God").

---

## T3.4 — Affect

### T3.4.1

**Question:** Does this characteristic engage the affective faculty — feeling and emotional experience — and if so, how?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Yes. The affective faculty is engaged in several registers. (1) *Fear as affective deterrent*: Exo 20:20 ("that the fear of him may be before you, that you may not sin") — fear of God is an affective state that prevents sinning. (2) *Social fear as sinning-enabler*: 1Sa 15:24 ("I feared the people") — fear of social consequence is the affective state that produces capitulation to sinning. (3) *Anger as sinning-risk*: Psa 4:4 ("be angry, and do not sin; ponder in your own hearts") — anger is the affective state that creates risk of sinning. (4) *Contempt as sinning-mode*: Isa 1:4 ("they have despised the Holy One of Israel") — despising is an affective act that functions as sinning.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The affect evidence is limited but present in the post-act realisation phase. Lev 5:5 ("when he realizes his guilt... and confesses") — the realisation of guilt involves an affective component: guilt-as-felt-state is the affective register of the cognitive recognition. Num 22:34 (Balaam — "I have sinned, for I did not know... if it is evil in your sight, I will turn back") — the offer to turn back carries an affective quality of contrition, though the text does not name the affective state explicitly. In VCG-02 (cultic-purification), affect is absent — the ritual purification of an altar or sanctuary is a procedural act without named inner emotional register.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The affective faculty is engaged in confession's shame and sorrow dimensions. Jer 3:25 — "Let us lie down in our shame, and let our dishonor cover us" — shame is the affective state that accompanies and frames the confession: the people are willing to accept shame as the appropriate inner-outer condition for what they have done. Dan 9:8 — "To us, O Lord, belongs open shame, to our kings, to our princes, and to our fathers, because we have sinned against you" — shame is specifically named as the appropriate affective consequence of the named sin. Lam 5:7 — "Our fathers sinned... and we bear their iniquities" — the emotional weight of bearing inherited iniquity carries grief-adjacent affect.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The affective faculty is engaged through its *distorted operation* in conscience suppression. Ecc 8:12 — the sinner who "prolongs his life" while "doing evil a hundred times" presumably experiences some form of positive affect (satisfaction, comfort) that reinforces the suppression — the absence of the fear of God means the negative affect (shame, fear) that should accompany sinning is absent. Jer 2:35 — "surely his anger has turned from me" — the person feels secure (positive affective state) where the appropriate state would be concerned awareness of divine anger. The affective faculty produces false peace where appropriate fear should exist.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The affective faculty is engaged in the dulling mode. 1Ki 16:31 — "as if it had been a light thing" — the affective register of moral gravity is dulled: Jeroboam's sins, which should produce moral revulsion or at least serious concern, produce only a light response. 2Ki 3:3's "clung to" (*davaq*) carries an attachment dimension — the will has an affective bond with the sinful course, making departure emotionally as well as volitionally difficult. 1Ti 5:20 — "so that the rest may stand in fear" — the public rebuke is aimed at producing the affect of fear in the community, implying that appropriate moral fear is what refusal to repent lacks.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The affective faculty is engaged through the *misdirected allegiance* that 2Ki 17:7 names. "Sinned against the Lord their God... and had *feared* other gods" — the fear-as-reverence is an affective-volitional state: their reverence, loyalty, and emotional allegiance has been transferred from the Lord to other gods. The habitual defection in this instance is constituted by an affective misdirection: the emotional-devotional capacity is operating toward wrong objects. Ecc 7:26 — "the sinner is taken" — the sinner's entrapment has an affective dimension: the seductive snare works through the affective pull of what the trap offers, and the sinner lacks the God-oriented affect that would enable escape.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The affective faculty is engaged through the relational provocation dimension. 1Ki 14:22 — "they provoked him to jealousy with their sins" — the community's transmitted sinning provokes God's jealousy: a relational-affective response from God to the breach of covenant loyalty. 1Ki 16:13 — "provoking the Lord God of Israel to anger with their idols" — the idolatrous direction transmitted through contagious sin produces the affective-relational provocation of God. On the community side, the affective allegiance (reverential fear/love) has been diverted: 2Ki 17:7 — "feared other gods" — the affective devotion is redirected through the transmitted sinning to wrong objects.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(S)*

**[CHAR-8]** S — The 9-verse corpus contains no explicit affective vocabulary in connection with political revolt. The characteristic is described through political-action language (rebelled, revolted, set up a king) without named affective states of the revolting parties.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The affective faculty is engaged through the emotional states that generate sinful speech. Eph 4:31 — slander is listed alongside "bitterness and wrath and anger and clamor" — the affective states of bitterness, wrath, and anger are the emotional context within which slander arises. Col 3:8 — "anger, wrath, malice, slander" — the emotional sequence culminates in slander. 1Ti 6:4 — "unhealthy craving for controversy" — a diseased affective appetite drives the slanderous speech. The characteristic is affectively driven: the corrupted emotional register generates the sinful words.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The affective faculty is engaged primarily through enticement. Jam 1:14 — "his own desire" — the desire-faculty is inherently affective: it is a faculty of appetite, longing, and motivational pull. 2Pe 2:14 — "insatiable for sin" — the insatiability is an affective state of unquenched appetition. 2Pe 2:18 — "sensual passions of the flesh" — the sensual passions are explicitly affective. Hypocrisy (Gal 2:13) engages affect through the social-belonging dimension — the desire for peer acceptance is an affective motivation. Apostasy (Act 21:21) engages affect through the covenantal allegiance that is being abandoned.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The affective faculty is engaged through the passions and desires that the condition activates. Rom 6:12 — "let not sin therefore reign in your mortal body, to make you obey its passions" — the condition operates through bodily passions as its affective instruments of rule; sin reigns by making the person obey affective desires rather than God. Rom 7:5 — "our sinful passions, aroused by the law, were at work in our members" — the sinful passions are the affective-level instruments through which the condition operates within the fleshly state. 2Ti 3:6 — "led astray by various passions" — the condition-burdened person is susceptible to multiple affective passions that lead them further from God.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The affective faculty is engaged through Eph 4:26 — "be angry and do not sin; do not let the sun go down on your anger" — anger is an affective state that can become sin if sustained; the verse engages the affective-to-sin transition. The broader corpus also implies affective engagement: the practice of sinning (Joh 8:34) involves affective desires that drive the sinning practices; the enslaving power operates through what the person is affectively drawn to.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — Yes — the record produces affective responses when conscience-accessible. Psa 38:18 — "I am sorry for my sin" — the record produces sorrow as its affective consequence; Neh 9:2 — "stood and confessed their sins and the iniquities of their fathers" — the corporate assembly's affective posture before the record is penitential standing. Psa 38:3 — the physical consequences of the record ("no health in my bones") produce what is implicitly an affective state of illness and distress. Eze 33:10 — "our sins are upon us, and we rot away because of them. How then can we live?" — the affective dimension is despair: the weight of the record produces hopelessness.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Yes — centrally on the receiving side. Luk 7:47 — "her sins, which are many, are forgiven — for she loved much. But he who is forgiven little, loves little" — love is the affective response that forgiveness received produces; the magnitude of love is proportional to the magnitude of forgiveness. Lev 23:27 — "you shall afflict yourselves" — the self-affliction on the Day of Atonement is an affective-penitential posture required on the seeking side. The characteristic thus engages affect at both ends: self-affliction in seeking; love in receiving.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The affective faculty is engaged through the doom-despair dimension. Jer 8:14 — "the Lord our God has doomed us to perish and has given us poisoned water to drink, because we have sinned against the Lord" — the corporate recognition of doom produces an affective state of hopelessness: "why do we sit still?" — paralysis and despair. Isa 64:5 — "and shall we be saved?" — the question expresses an affective state of uncertainty about whether salvation is possible after the long duration of the sinning-condition.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Yes — on both the sinner's and the observer's side. (1) *Sinner's affect*: Isa 33:14 — "sinners in Zion are afraid; trembling has seized the godless" — dread and trembling are the affective response of the sinner-identity confronting divine holiness; Luk 18:13 — beating the breast is an affective-penitential act expressing grief over the sinner-identity; (2) *Observer's affect*: Pro 23:17 — "let not your heart envy sinners" — the observer's affective response to the sinner's apparent prosperity is envy; Psa 26:9 — "do not sweep my soul away with sinners" — the affective response is fear of being swept into the sinner's fate.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Yes — as one of its primary inner-being dimensions. Ezr 9:6 — "I am ashamed and blush to lift my face to you, my God" — shame and blush are the affective responses to guilt-recognition; Ezr 9:6 — this is the corpus's most explicit affect description; Gen 4:13 — "my punishment is greater than I can bear" — despair/overwhelm is the affective experience of guilt-as-punitive-weight; Hos 5:5 — "shall stumble in his guilt" — the stumbling includes an affective dimension of moral unsteadiness. 1Sa 25:31 — pu.qah — "pangs of conscience" — the term directly names the affective-experiential dimension of conscience-stagger under guilt.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Yes — through the weight-experience and the sorrow dimension. Psa 38:4 — "like a heavy burden, they are too heavy for me" — the iniquity-weight produces an affective experience of overwhelm; Psa 38:18 — "I am sorry for my sin" — the affect of sorrow accompanies confession of iniquity; Ezr 9:6 — "I am ashamed and blush to lift my face to you" — shame is the primary affective response to the recognised accumulation. Psa 40:12 — "my heart fails me" — the iniquities overtaking the person produce an affective collapse.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Yes — through the love-of-transgression dimension and the weight-grief dimension. (1) *Affection toward transgression*: Pro 17:19 — "whoever loves transgression loves strife" — the inner affective capacity of love is directed toward transgression itself; the positive emotional orientation toward transgression is a distinct inner condition; (2) *Grief and collapse under transgression's weight*: Lam 1:22 — "my heart is faint" — the affective collapse under accumulated transgressions; Eze 33:10 — "how then can we live?" — the desperate affective response to the burden of accumulated transgressions.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Yes — through the grief-of-witness and the desolation-of-victim dimensions. (1) *Grief of witness*: Ezr 9:4 — Ezra "sat appalled" and fasted; Ezr 10:6 — "mourning over the faithlessness of the exiles, neither eating bread nor drinking water" — the spiritually attuned person's affective response to witnessing faithlessness is deep grief; (2) *Desolation of victim*: Lam 1:2 — "she weeps bitterly in the night, with tears on her cheeks; all her friends have dealt treacherously with her; they have become her enemies" — weeping bitterly as the victim's affective experience; (3) *Desire as faithlessness-driver*: Pro 11:6 — "the treacherous are taken captive by their lust" — the craving-affect is the inner driver of the faithlessness.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Yes — in both the affective-attraction-to-perversion and the affective-distress-under-perversion directions. (1) *Affective attraction*: Pro 2:14 — "who rejoice in doing evil and delight in the perverseness of evil" — the will and emotion are positively oriented toward what is morally inverted; delight and rejoicing are the named affective states toward perversity; (2) *Affective distress*: Psa 38:6 — "I am utterly bowed down and prostrate; all the day I go about mourning" — the person under the weight of their own perversity mourns; the affect is grief and prostration.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Yes — through the God-hatred of injustice and the human affective responses. (1) *Divine affect*: Isa 61:8 — "I the Lord love justice; I hate robbery and wrong" — God's love and hatred as the defining affective standard; (2) *Righteous person's fretful response*: Psa 37:1 — "fret not yourself because of evildoers; be not envious of wrongdoers" — the inner fretfulness and envy that injustice's apparent prosperity evokes in the righteous; (3) *Victim's pleading response*: Job 6:29 — "let no injustice be done... my vindication is at stake" — the urgency of the plea; Psa 43:1 — "deliver me from the deceitful and unjust man" — the affect of urgent appeal.

---

### T3.4.2

**Question:** Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair affect in the person?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Wilful sinning *exploits* affect in the service of the will's turning against God or persons. Fear of people (1Sa 15:24) is the affective lever through which the will is moved to sin against God. Anger (Psa 4:4) is the affective energy that, unchecked by pondering, produces sinful action. Contempt/despising (Isa 1:4) is an affective posture that constitutes sinning. The pattern: sinning does not bypass affect but weaponises available affect (fear, anger, contempt) in the direction of the wrong. Conversely, the affective deterrent — fear of God (Exo 20:20) — is the affect that the will needs to be kept operative to resist sinning.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(S)*

**[CHAR-2]** S — The 18-verse corpus provides insufficient evidence to determine whether unintentional sinning systematically enables, deepens, bypasses, or impairs the affective faculty. The realisation-of-guilt affective register (Lev 5:5) suggests that affect is activated post-disclosure, but the corpus does not characterise the affective state at the moment of sinning (where affect would likely be neutral, since the moral reality was not registered).

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Genuine confession *engages and accepts* the appropriate affect — shame (Jer 3:25; Dan 9:8) — without being paralysed by it. The confession of shame is not itself the goal but the honest affective accompaniment of naming sin. Mic 7:9 — bearing the Lord's indignation — shows the confessor accepting the affective weight of God's displeasure as appropriate to the sin named. Hollow confession (Deu 1:41; Exo 9:27) bypasses the affective dimension — the people immediately act without accepting shame; Pharaoh confesses without the affect of contrition.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Conscience suppression *distorts* the affective faculty: it produces or sustains *inappropriate positive affect* (false peace, false security — Jer 2:35) in place of the *appropriate negative affect* (fear, shame, guilt) that moral self-awareness should generate. 1Cor 15:34 — the "drunken stupor" is an affective state of false ease rather than moral alertness. Paul's appeal to shame ("I say this to your shame") aims to correct the affective distortion by introducing the appropriate negative affect.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Refusal to repent *distorts* the affective faculty: it dulls appropriate negative affect (fear of God, revulsion at sin — 1Ki 16:31 — the light-thing assessment shows dulled moral affect) and may generate inappropriate affective attachment to the sinful course (2Ki 3:3 — "clung to"). The dulling of appropriate moral affect enables the will to continue; the affective attachment to the sinful course makes departure emotionally costly. Both distortions serve the refusal.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Habitual defection *misdirects* the affective faculty: the reverential fear that belongs to God (2Ki 17:7 — "feared other gods" instead of the Lord) has been redirected toward wrong objects. The capacity for reverence and allegiance remains operative but is directed toward the sinning pattern and its objects rather than toward God. Over time, the affective bond with the sinning pattern deepens (Exo 9:34 — Pharaoh's hardening cycle; Hos 8:11 — altars producing more sinning), making the emotional investment in the sinning course progressively harder to reverse.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Contagious sin *misdirects* the affective faculty: the community's God-directed reverence and allegiance is redirected toward idols (2Ki 17:7; 1Ki 16:13/26 — "provoked the Lord with their idols"). The affective capacity for worship, devotion, and reverence remains operative but is aimed at wrong objects. The authority medium makes this affective misdirection effective: the community's affective trust in the leader enables the redirection without significant resistance. The idolatrous allegiance that contagious sin produces is a deep affective investment — "feared other gods" (2Ki 17:7) names a genuine affective orientation, not merely outward compliance.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(S)*

**[CHAR-8]** S — No evidence. The corpus is silent on the affective dimension of political revolt.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Sinful speech *expresses and deepens* the affective states that generate it. Eph 4:31 and Col 3:8 present slander as the verbal output of bitterness, wrath, and anger — speaking the slander expresses the anger and may deepen it (the act of articulating hostile speech consolidates the hostile affective state). 2Ti 3:2 — "lovers of self" as the root affective orientation — self-love as the foundational affect that generates the abusive disposition.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*
