This is why the Hiphil causative is the perfect grammatical form: "caused to sin" names a real causal influence that respects the recipient's volitional reality while acknowledging the leader's role in shaping it. The community's sinning after contagious sin transmission is genuinely their own sinning (God holds them accountable — 1Ki 14:22) while also being causally connected to the leader's transmission.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The volitional pattern confirms that political revolt is a *full exercise of collective volitional agency* at the community-political level: the revolting community makes a decisive collective decision to break submission and act on it. The pa.sha root's deliberate-transgressive character confirms the volitional quality: the revolt is not accidental or coerced but a deliberate chosen act of the community's political will. This volitional character is what gives political revolt its moral weight — it is transgression because it is wilfully chosen, not merely circumstantially produced.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The volitional pattern reveals that sinful speech is *volitionally avoidable* (Jude 9 — Michael withholds it) but *volitionally self-binding* once practised (1Ti 6:4 — craving grows with use; VCG-03 — beast's will fully bound to its blasphemous character). The will is the decisive faculty: the same situation that Jude 9's Michael navigates with restraint is the situation where a lesser-willed creature might produce sinful speech. Restraint is possible — the will can withhold the word — but requires the inner condition of creaturely humility (deferring to God: "The Lord rebuke you" — Jude 9).

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The volitional pattern reveals that the three mechanisms stand in a spectrum relative to explicit volition. Apostasy is most volitionally explicit (a deliberate departure — Act 21:21). Hypocrisy is volitionally present but socially pressured (the choice is made under group conformity pressure — Gal 2:13). Enticement is least explicitly volitional — operating through the desire-faculty that pre-empts deliberate choice (Jam 1:14). This spectrum reveals why enticement is the most insidious mechanism: it captures the will through a route that bypasses its deliberate-decision mode.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The volitional pattern reveals that sin as universal condition creates a constitutive *volitional captivity with preserved volitional identity*. The person under the condition still wills, chooses, and acts (the condition does not abolish the will) but the will's effective freedom toward God has been compromised by the enslavement: Rom 7:17/20 — "it is no longer I who do it" — the "I" remains (identity preserved) but effective agency is overridden (the enslavement operates through the person's members). This subtlety is unique to CHAR-11: the condition does not destroy the will but captures its directional freedom, producing the characteristic divided-person experience of willing the good while the condition produces the evil.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The volitional pattern reveals that sin's enslaving power produces *directional captivity without volitional elimination*: the enslaved will continues to function as a will (choosing, acting) but its free range of motion has been restricted to the sin-domain. Joh 8:34's "slave to sin" names the restriction precisely: a slave still wills and acts but within the bounds of the master's domain. The liberation (1Jo 3:9 — "cannot keep on sinning") creates a counter-directional impossibility: the born-of-God person's will is constitutionally directed away from sustained sinning, just as the enslaved will is constitutionally directed within it.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The volitional pattern reveals that the divine record is the precise moral archive of the will's history: each volitional choice either adds to or (through repentance) moves toward erasure of the record. VCG-04's 46 verses of kings who "walked in" and "did not depart from" the sins of Jeroboam are the clearest evidence: the record is constituted by sustained volitional direction. The will's "turning away" (Eze 18:21) is the key to addressing the record: not a cognitive acknowledgement alone but a volitional redirection that enables divine erasure.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The volitional pattern reveals the characteristic's paradoxical structure: the seeking requires volition (repent, go up, believe), yet the received forgiveness produces a restored volition (alive, Spirit-empowered) that was not fully available before seeking. The will must be exercised in seeking (with whatever partial freedom remains) before the forgiveness received restores the will's fuller freedom. This is the characteristic's volitional dynamic: partial will exercises toward God → forgiveness received → fuller will restored.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The volitional pattern reveals the generational pattern's dynamic: the inherited sinning condition creates a volitional default-direction toward sin (each generation's "still sinning" — Psa 78:17/32) while the potential for heart-turning remains (1Ki 8:47 — "if they turn their heart"). Generational sin is thus not volitional determinism but *volitional habituation*: the will's default is shaped by the inherited pattern, but the capacity for the decisive turn (repentance — 1Ki 8:47) is not eliminated. The will must actively turn against the inherited direction rather than merely choosing differently.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The volitional pattern reveals a distinctive feature of CHAR-16 absent from other M10 characteristics: Luk 6:32/33/34's portrait of the sinner as having *limited but real moral capacity*. Sinners are not presented as totally depraved (without any good volition) but as volitionally confined to reciprocal self-interest. This is the corpus's most nuanced inner-being finding: the sinner has genuine inner goodness within a restricted radius (loving those who love them is real love; Luk 6:33 — "sinners do the same" treats their good-doing as genuine). The sinner-identity limits the will's reach, not its reality.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The volitional pattern reveals that guilt-as-inner-state is a volitional hinge-point: when the conscience's guilt-recognition engages correctly, it redirects the will toward restitution and confession (Lev 6:4; Num 5:7); when it is suppressed or overwhelmed, the will either persists in guilt-accumulation (2Ch 33:23) or is paralysed by the punitive weight (Gen 4:13). The guilt-state itself does not determine the volitional response — the will's direction (toward remedy or away from it) is what determines whether guilt becomes productive or destructive.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The volitional pattern reveals CHAR-18's most significant inner-being dynamic: accumulated iniquity progressively constrains the will that generated it. The will begins as free agent (choosing iniquity — Eze 14:3), produces the accumulation, and is then bound by what it chose (Pro 5:22 — "held fast in the cords"). This self-generated volitional bondage makes the removal of iniquity require external divine action (Zec 3:4 — "I have taken your iniquity away"; Mic 7:19 — "he will tread our iniquities underfoot") rather than merely the will's renewed effort — the will is no longer fully free to extract itself.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The volitional pattern reveals CHAR-19's central inner-being dynamic: the will that freely chose to cross the boundary is progressively enslaved by the transgression it chose. Psa 19:13 — "great transgression" seeking dominion over the will; Pro 29:6 — ensnared. This self-generated volitional bondage distinguishes CHAR-19 from all other M10 characteristics: the will is both the agent that creates the characteristic (the deliberate crossing) and the primary victim of its sustained operation (the ensnaring). The remedy requires the will to turn (Eze 18:30) — but this turning itself requires divine assistance (Eze 18:31 — "make yourselves a new heart and new spirit").

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The volitional pattern reveals CHAR-20's most characteristic inner-being dynamic: the will that freely chose to betray is progressively enslaved by what it chose. 2Ch 28:22's Ahaz — distress producing more faithlessness — is the most extreme portrait of this dynamic: the will has become so constitutively set in the faithless direction that environmental pressure drives it further rather than correcting it. This volitional-hardening is distinct from CHAR-19's volitional-ensnaring (Pro 29:6 — ensnared in transgression) in that CHAR-20's hardening is specifically of the covenant-allegiance faculty rather than general will-bondage.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The volitional pattern reveals the same trajectory as CHAR-19 and CHAR-20: the will freely embraces perversity (Pro 2:14) and is progressively enslaved by it (2Pe 2:19). CHAR-21's distinctive contribution is the enslavement-mechanism precision: "whatever overcomes a person, to that he is enslaved" (2Pe 2:19) — the general principle that mastery produces slavery confirms that perversion's capture of the will is not unique to perversion but follows the universal mechanism of conquest-produces-slavery.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The volitional pattern reveals CHAR-22's distinctive trajectory: from volitional act (Eze 3:20 — commits injustice) through fixed character-scale (Luk 16:10 — dishonest in all) to shameless insensibility (Zep 3:5 — knows no shame). The will begins as the agent of injustice and progressively becomes the captive of the unjust character it has formed. The reversibility remains open until judgment (Eze 33:15 — the wicked can still stop doing injustice and live) but grows more constitutionally embedded with each sustained choice.

---

## T3.7 — Agency

### T3.7.1

**Question:** Does this characteristic engage the agency faculty — the capacity to act, initiate, and make happen — and if so, how?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Agency is fully engaged. Wilful sinning is an act — something done, initiated, made to happen by the person. The verbal grammar of the corpus (cha.ta as action verb — committed, sinned, sins) confirms this. Gen 43:9 and 44:32 (Judah voluntarily assuming agency for Benjamin's safety) show agency in the responsibility-for-another mode. Jos 7:20 (Achan — "this is what I did") shows agency as the defining element of the confession: the act is personally initiated. The deterrence corpus (VCG-03) shows agency being disciplined: Job 31:30 ("I have not let my mouth sin") — the "not let" is an agency-restraint act.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Agency is fully engaged in the sinning act (the person did something — Lev 4:2; 1Sa 14:33) and in the remedy sequence (the person brings an offering — Num 15:27; Balaam offers to turn back — Num 22:34). The person is the agent of both the inadvertent sin and the remedy. Lev 5:5 ("when he realizes his guilt... and confesses") — the confession is an agency-act: the person is the active subject of both realization and confession.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Yes. Agency is engaged in two ways. (1) *Claiming agency for the sin*: 1Ch 21:17 — "Was it not I who gave command to number the people? It is I who have sinned and done great evil" — the confessor claims their own agency as the source of the sin, refusing to transfer responsibility. 2Sa 24:17 — "Behold, I have sinned, and I have done wickedly. But these sheep, what have they done?" — distinguishing the confessor's agency from the people's non-agency. (2) *Exercising agency in the confession act*: Luk 15:18 — "I will arise and go to my father" — the confession involves a deliberate agentive movement (arising and going) that enacts the inner resolution.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Agency is fully engaged in conscience suppression — the person continues to act, sin, and claim innocence. Jer 2:35 — the person acts on the claim "I am innocent" by assuming divine anger has passed and proceeding accordingly. Ecc 8:12 — the sinner who "does evil a hundred times" is exercising full agency in the sinning. The characteristic is not passivity but active sinning-without-conscience-awareness. Agency is the vehicle through which conscience suppression manifests in the external world.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Agency is fully engaged throughout. The Kings corpus shows kings who continue to act — governing, walking, serving — in the direction of the sinful course: 1Ki 16:31 — Ahab "went and served Baal and worshiped him" — his agency is fully operational, directed toward idolatry. The sinful course does not merely persist by default; agents are actively walking in it (2Ki 13:6 — "walked in them"). Heb 10:26 — "go on sinning deliberately" — the present tense going-on names continuous agentive action.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Agency is fully engaged — the habitually sinning person continues to act, to build, to fear, to pursue. 1Ki 16:31 (adjacent — CHAR-5) and 2Ki 17:7 show the community actively "fearing other gods" — a full exercise of agentive devotion directed toward wrong objects. Hos 8:11 — "Ephraim has multiplied altars" — the multiplying of altars is an agentive act: the community is actively building, investing, and institutionalising the sinning pattern. The habitual sinner's agency is fully operational; it is simply directed toward the sinning course and the structures that support it.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Agency is fully engaged in contagious sin at both ends. Leader's agency: 1Ki 16:2 — "you have walked in the way of Jeroboam and have made my people Israel to sin" — the leader's agentive actions (walking, making-to-sin) are the mechanism of transmission; Jer 32:35 — "they built the high places" — constructive agentive action building the sinning structure. Community's agency: 1Ki 14:22 — "Judah did what was evil in the sight of the Lord, and they provoked him to jealousy with their sins" — the community is agentively sinning, not merely being passive recipients; their agency is active in the transmitted sinning direction.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — Agency is fully engaged. The political revolts in the corpus are agentive acts: "Moab rebelled" (2Ki 1:1), "Edom revolted... and set up a king of their own" (2Ki 8:20), "Israel has been in rebellion" (1Ki 12:19) — all name communities as active agents. The constructive act of 2Ki 8:20 — "set up a king of their own" — is particularly agency-rich: not only breaking from but building alternative governance. Political revolt is constitutively agentive.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Agency is fully engaged — sinful speech is an agentive speech-act. Mat 26:65 — "He has *uttered* blasphemy" — the utterance is an agentive act attributed to Jesus (falsely) or to anyone who speaks sinfully. Rev 13:5/6 — "it *opened* its mouth... It *opened* its mouth to utter blasphemies" — the beast's agentive speech-opening is deliberate and sustained. Jude 9 — Michael "did not *presume* to pronounce" — the withholding of agency from the speech-act is what prevents sinful speech.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — Agency is engaged differently across the three mechanisms. (1) *Enticement*: the enticers of 2Pe 2:14/18 are fully agentive — they deliberately entice, their hearts are trained, they actively target unsteady souls. The enticed person's agency is being captured and redirected. (2) *Hypocrisy*: Gal 2:13 — "acted hypocritically along with him" — the acting-alongside is an agentive choice; Barnabas "was led astray" — his agency was captured by the collective hypocrisy. (3) *Apostasy*: Act 21:21 — "forsake Moses" — the forsaking is an agentive act of departure.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — Agency is fully engaged — but the condition operates by redirecting rather than eliminating it. Rom 6:13 — "do not present your members to sin as instruments for unrighteousness" — the agency of presenting the members is real; the person actively presents them (the presenting is a genuine agentive act). The condition captures this agency and deploys it toward sin. Rom 6:16 — "whoever you present yourselves as obedient slaves" — the act of presenting is agentive; the condition's enslavement operates through real agentive presentations, not through the abolition of agency.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — Agency is engaged through its distorted form under enslavement. Joh 8:34 — "everyone who practices sin is a slave to sin" — the practice of sinning is an agentive act (the person is doing the sinning); but the agency that generates the enslavement is the very agency that becomes enslaved. The practicing-sinner is agentively sinning while that agency progressively loses its free directional range. Rom 6:1 — "continue in sin" — the continuation is an agentive ongoing state; the enslaved person's agency operates within the sin-domain.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — Agency is engaged as both the generator and the addresser of the record. (1) *Agency generating the record*: Isa 5:18 — "who draw sin as with cart ropes" — the active, effortful drawing of sin toward the self is a fully agentive act that creates the record; Isa 30:1 — "who carry out a plan... that they may add sin to sin" — the deliberate agentive execution of plans against God accumulates the record. (2) *Agency addressing the record*: Num 5:7 — "he shall confess his sin that he has committed. And he shall make full restitution" — the agentive confession and restitution are the human side of the record-addressing mechanism.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Agency is engaged through the seeking acts. 2Ch 29:23 — "they laid their hands on them" — the assembly's agentive act of hand-laying; 2Ch 29:24 — "the priests slaughtered them and made a sin offering" — the priestly agentive acts. Act 2:38 — "repent and be baptized" — both repentance (inner agentive turn) and baptism (outer agentive submission) are acts of the person's agency. Exo 32:30/31 — Moses's agentive intercession: "I will go up to the Lord" and his verbal naming of the sin before God.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Yes — through both rebellion and confession. (1) *Agency in rebellion*: Psa 78:17 — "rebelling against the Most High in the desert" — the rebellion is an agentive act against God; (2) *Agency in confession*: 1Ki 8:47 — "turn their heart... and repent and plead" — the turning, repentance, and pleading are all agentive acts through which the community addresses the inherited condition; Jer 8:14 — "we have sinned against the Lord" — the corporate acknowledgment is an agentive speech-act.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Agency is engaged through the sinner's active expression of their identity. Heb 12:3 — "sinners directed such hostility against himself" — the sinner's agency is expressed in active opposition against Christ; the hostility is an agentive act. Jude 15 — "all their deeds of ungodliness... and the harsh things that ungodly sinners have spoken against him" — the deeds and speech are agentive expressions of the sinner-identity. Pro 1:10 — "if sinners entice you" — the sinners are agents actively recruiting others into their identity-pattern.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Agency is engaged through the restitution-demands that guilt recognition generates. Lev 6:4 — "realized his guilt and will restore what he took" — the guilt-recognition produces agentive restitution; Num 5:7 — "he shall confess... shall make full restitution... giving it to him to whom he did the wrong" — the guilt-state drives a series of agentive acts (confession, restitution, giving). 1Sa 25:24 — Abigail's "on me alone, my lord, be the guilt" — a proactive agentive assumption of another's guilt-liability. Jer 3:13 — "only acknowledge your guilt" — the acknowledgment is an agentive act of moral self-declaration.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Agency is engaged through the active expression of iniquity and the active pursuit of its remedy. (1) *Agency in iniquity's enactment*: Isa 59:3 — "your hands are defiled with blood and your fingers with iniquity" — the hands are the active members of iniquity's enactment; Isa 5:18 — "those who draw iniquity with cords of falsehood, who draw sin as with cart ropes" — active, purposeful pursuit of iniquity; (2) *Agency in iniquity's remedy*: Lev 26:40 — "if they confess their iniquity" — the verbal act of confession is an agentive decision; Hos 14:2 — "take with you words and return to the Lord" — the return-initiative requires agentive action.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Agency is engaged through both the transgression's active commission and the active pursuit of its resolution. (1) *Agency in transgression's commission*: Isa 5:18 (CHAR-18 cross, but relevant here) — "those who draw iniquity with cords... sin as with cart ropes" — active, purposeful pursuit; Amos 4:4 — "come to Bethel and transgress" — ironic invitation to purposeful transgressive action; (2) *Agency in transgression's resolution*: Pro 28:13 — "he who confesses and forsakes them" — the forsaking is an agentive act; Eze 18:31 — "cast away from you all the transgressions" — the casting-away is a commanded agentive act of the will.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Agency is engaged through both the commission and the communal extension of faithlessness. (1) *Individual agentive commission*: Eze 17:20 — "for the treachery he has committed against me" — the specific agentive act named as the ground of judgment; 1Ch 10:13 — "he broke faith with the Lord in that he did not keep the command" — the specific agentive choices constituting the faithlessness; (2) *Communal agentive extension*: Num 31:16 — "caused the people of Israel to act treacherously against the Lord in the incident of Peor" — Balaam's agency incited communal faithlessness; Jos 7:1 — Achan's individual agency attributed to all Israel.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Agency is engaged through both active commission and the reversal to passivity. (1) *Active agency*: Pro 6:14 — "devises evil, continually sowing discord" — active agentive commission; Rev 19:2 — "the great prostitute who corrupted the earth with her immorality" — active agentive corruption reaching to cosmic scale; Isa 32:7 — "plans wicked schemes to ruin the poor" — deliberately agentive; (2) *Passive captivity*: 2Pe 2:19 — "slaves of corruption" — the agency is captured; Jude 10 — "destroyed by all that they understand instinctively" — the active agent has become the passive victim of their own instinct.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Agency is central — CHAR-22 is defined by what is *done* toward others. (1) *Active agentive wrongdoing*: Psa 58:2 — "your hands deal out violence on earth"; Rev 19:2 (adjacent) — corrupting the earth; Rev 18:5 — crimes heaped high; (2) *Agentive harm*: Job 29:17 — "I broke the fangs of the unrighteous" — the unjust person as a predatory agent; 2Sa 7:10 — "violent men shall afflict them no more" — the violent as active agents of harm; (3) *Agentive civic injustice*: Mic 3:10 — "who build Zion with blood" — agency deployed toward constructing unjust social structures.

---

### T3.7.2

**Question:** Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair agency in the person?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Wilful sinning initially *expresses* full agency but progressively *impairs* it through habituation. The single act exercises agency in the sinning direction; the accumulated pattern (Hos 4:7; 13:2) shows agency increasingly captured by the sinning pattern. 1Jo 2:1 ("if anyone does sin, we have an advocate") frames the sinning believer as still an agent (not a determined automaton) but one who needs intervention — the advocate — to restore their standing before God. The impairment is not elimination of agency but its gradual colonisation by sinning's logic.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Unintentional sinning does not impair agency — the person remains a fully capable moral agent throughout. The characteristic reveals that agency can produce moral liability even without deliberate intent. Lev 5:1 ("he shall bear his iniquity") — bearing the iniquity is an agency-claim: the person is accountable for the iniquity their agency produced, even inadvertently. The fact that a remedy is prescribed and the person is obligated to pursue it (Num 15:27/28) confirms that agency remains fully operative post-sin.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Genuine confession *restores and clarifies* agency: by owning the sin as one's own act, the confessor names themselves as the responsible agent, which is the foundation for seeking remedy and making different choices going forward. 2Sa 24:10/17 — David's agency claimed → petition for mercy → the will now oriented toward remedy. Hollow confession (Deu 1:41) shows agency *misdirected*: the people claim agency for their past sin but immediately redirect their agency toward autonomous military action rather than following God's direction.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Conscience suppression *disinhibits agency*: by removing the internal check that conscience provides, it allows the will and agency to proceed without the moral restraint that conscience would impose. Ecc 8:12 — "does evil a hundred times" — the hundred-times sinning is possible because agency operates without the conscience's inhibiting signal. The disinhibition is not a positive enablement but a dangerous one: the agency that operates without conscience is the agency that produces the accumulated judgment of Jer 2:35 and the accumulated evil of Ecc 8:12.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Refusal to repent *misdirects* agency without impairing its operational capacity. The person who refuses to repent remains a fully capable agent — they act, govern, worship, sin — but all their agentive outputs are directed within the sinful course rather than away from it. Over time, the accumulated agentive outputs of the sinful course (the actions performed while not departing) constitute the person's life-trajectory, making departure increasingly costly: 2Ki 15:18 — "all his days" — an entire lifetime's agency invested in the sinful course.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Habitual defection *misdirects and gradually constrains* agency. The community multiplying altars (Hos 8:11) is exercising full agency — building, creating, institutionalising — but in service of the sinning pattern. Over time, the institutions built by the sinning agency (the altars) constrain future agency by making the sinning course the established social path: the altars become the default context within which future agentive choices are made. Agency builds the structures that then constrain future agency — the investment of agency in the sinning pattern progressively narrows the agentive space available for departure from it.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Contagious sin *misdirects* agency at the leader level (the leader's agency is deployed toward sinning transmission) and *shapes* the community's agency toward the sinning direction. The community's agency is not removed but its default directionality is shaped by the sustained transmitted sinning. 1Ki 14:22 — "Judah did" and "they provoked" — their agency is fully active; it has been shaped toward sinning by the transmitted direction. The corporate sinning of 1Ki 14:22 is evidenced as more than the sum of individual acts — it is the community's collective agency operating within the sinning framework the leader established.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — Political revolt *exercises and asserts* agency: the revolting community claims full political agency by declaring independence and establishing its own governing structure (2Ki 8:20). The revolt is itself an assertion of agency: "we will govern ourselves" rather than submit to external governance. The characteristic thus demonstrates the full operation of community-level political agency in the act of revolt.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Sinful speech *misdirects* the agency of speech: the agentive capacity to speak is deployed toward sinful content rather than righteous speech. The characteristic does not impair speech-agency (the person remains a speaking agent) but directs it toward harmful, presumptuous, or defiant content. Rev 13:5's "it was allowed to exercise authority" — even the beast's blasphemous speech-agency operates within a divinely permitted scope — confirming that the agency is real, its content is the problem.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — (1) *Enticement misdirects and progressively captures agency*: the enticers deploy full agency toward wrong ends (2Pe 2:14/18); the enticed person's agency is progressively captured by the desire-exploitation mechanism. (2) *Hypocrisy misdirects agency*: the agentive capacity to identify and act from authentic identity is redirected toward performed identity (Gal 2:13). (3) *Apostasy expresses agency*: the departure from covenantal commitment is a full exercise of the will's agency — the person acts to stand away from what they had committed to (Act 21:21).

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The condition *captures and misdirects* agency. The person's agency remains operative (they present members — Rom 6:13; they obey a master — Rom 6:16; they sin — Rom 2:12/3:23) but the direction of that agency has been captured by the condition. Rom 6:20 — "you were free in regard to righteousness" — the former slaves of sin had their agency fully occupied by the sinful domain, leaving no agentive freedom for righteousness. The impairment is directional: agency toward sin is preserved; agency toward God is restricted by the condition's enslavement.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — Sin's enslaving power *misdirects and progressively constrains* agency. The enslaved person's agency is fully operative within the sin-domain (they practice sinning — Joh 8:34) but cannot exercise agency toward God (the enslaving power forecloses God-directed agentive freedom). Gal 3:22 — "imprisoned everything under sin" — the imprisonment names the constraint on agency's range: the will's agency cannot move toward the promise through human effort; only faith can receive the promise.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The record *deepens through agentive accumulation*: Isa 5:18 — actively drawing sin with cart ropes names maximum agency in record-creation; Isa 30:1 — "add sin to sin" — deliberate accumulation. However, the record *does not eliminate agency*: the will can turn (Eze 18:21) and the person can confess and make restitution (Num 5:7) — these are genuine agentive responses to the record. The record is the history of the agency's past choices; it does not foreclose future agentive change.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The characteristic *requires and then restores* agency. The seeking requires genuine agentive acts (hand-laying, repentance, belief, intercession — as above); without agency the seeking cannot begin. The received forgiveness then restores deeper agency: Col 2:13 — made alive (the constitutional restoration that enables full agentive participation in the God-relationship); Act 2:38 — Spirit received (the agent of empowered action following forgiveness).

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The generational pattern *misdirects* agency: the community's agentive capacity is deployed toward rebellion (Psa 78:17 — actively rebelling) rather than toward covenant-faithfulness. The "still more" (Psa 78:17) indicates escalating agentive investment in the wrong direction. However, the possibility of corporate repentance (1Ki 8:47) confirms that agency is not eliminated — it can be redirected through the heart-turning act.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The sinner-identity *redirects* agency toward self and against God. Gen 13:13 — "great sinners against the Lord" — the agency is actively deployed against God; Heb 12:3 — hostility against Christ — the agency is deployed as opposition. The sinner's agency is not impaired (they act with evident purposefulness — enticing, speaking harshly, rebelling) but its orientation is against God rather than toward him. This is consistent with T3.6's finding: the sinner has real volitional and agentive capacity, but it is oriented within the self-interest radius and against the divine.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — When conscience is engaged, guilt-state *activates and directs* agency toward remedial acts. The Lev 4/5/6 sequences show guilt-recognition consistently producing agentive responses: bringing offerings (Lev 5:6 — "shall bring to the Lord his compensation"), confessing (Lev 5:5; Num 5:7), making restitution (Lev 6:4; Num 5:7). The guilt-state does not eliminate agency but provides the moral impetus that redirects it from self-serving action toward other-repairing action.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Accumulated iniquity *progressively impairs agency* through the ensnaring mechanism. Pro 5:22 — "he is held fast in the cords of his sin" — the agency that once freely chose iniquity is now constrained; Isa 64:6 — "our iniquities, like the wind, take us away" — the iniquities become an external force acting on the person who was formerly the agent; Isa 64:7 — "have made us melt in the hand of our iniquities" — the hand of iniquity has replaced the person's own agentive hand; the person is now acted upon by their own accumulated crimes.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Sustained transgression *ensnares and impairs* agency. Pro 12:13 — "ensnared by the transgression of his lips" — the transgressor's own acts become the trap constraining future agency; Pro 29:6 — "ensnared in his transgression" — the evil man's free agency is captured by what he has done. Psa 19:13 — "let them not have dominion over me" — the threat is that transgression assumes agentive control over the person. The remedy restores agency: Eze 18:31 — "cast away from you" — the casting-away is the agentive recovery act made possible by the new heart.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Faithlessness *captures and corrupts* agency through lust. Pro 11:6 — "the treacherous are taken captive by their lust" — the agency that began as free covenant-breaking becomes captivity to the lust that drove the betrayal; the traitor's agency is no longer free but driven by craving. Pro 13:2 — "the desire of the treacherous is for violence" — the settled desire has taken over the agentive orientation. The individual's faithlessness also compromises community agency (Jos 7:1 — one person's act attributed to all) — the faithless person's agency reaches beyond the individual to implicate the community.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Perversion *redirects and ultimately captures* agency. The active devising (Pro 6:14 — "devises evil continually") and scheming (Isa 32:7 — "plans wicked schemes") show agency being deployed toward corruption. 2Pe 2:19's "slaves of corruption" and Jude 10's "destroyed by instinct" show the endpoint: the formerly agentive person has become the captive and victim of what they once actively chose. The agency trajectory confirms CHAR-21's self-destructive character: active corruption produces passive destruction.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Injustice *constitutes* a stable agentive identity. Luk 16:10 — consistent dishonesty across all scales — the agency is consistently deployed toward wrong; Job 15:16 — drinking injustice like water — the agency operates with wrongdoing as its natural medium. Pro 22:8 — "whoever sows injustice will reap calamity, and the rod of his fury will fail" — the agentive power ("rod of his fury") is itself disabled by the injustice's consequence. Agency in CHAR-22 is not impaired but is constitutively directed toward wrongdoing.

---

### T3.7.3

**Question:** What does the pattern of engagement or non-engagement with agency reveal about the nature of this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — The agency pattern confirms that wilful sinning is a *responsible act* — the corpus never excuses it as compelled or inevitable (even Saul's self-excuse in 1Sa 15:24 is narratively presented as inadequate: Samuel does not accept it as a valid defence). The moral accountability in Eze 18:4/20, the personal confession in Jos 7:20, and the deterrence corpus all presuppose that agency is real and operative. The characteristic is constituted by genuine agency — which is precisely why it is morally serious.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — The agency pattern confirms that moral accountability in the Levitical framework is based on *agency*, not on *intentionality*. The fact that the person acted (exercised agency) is sufficient to create moral liability, regardless of the inner quality of the will at the moment of action. This is a foundational principle of Lev 4:2 and Num 15:27/28: agency creates liability; intentionality differentiates the *kind* and *scale* of the remedy required, not whether a remedy is required at all.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — The agency pattern reveals that confession is an *agency-claiming event*: it requires the person to identify themselves as the agent of the sin rather than displacing responsibility. This is why genuine confession is morally demanding — it requires claiming full personal or corporate agency for what was done (1Ch 21:17; 2Sa 24:17; Dan 9:5 — "we have sinned"). The moral demand is proportional to how clearly the agency-claim is owned. Hollow confession (Deu 1:41) shows the failure: the verbal claim of agency for the sin does not produce ownership of agency for the remedy.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — The agency pattern reveals that conscience suppression is *an agency-disinhibition event*: it does not restrict the person's capacity to act but removes the moral framework within which acting should occur. The person's agency becomes fully operational without its governing moral check — which is precisely why conscience suppression is dangerous. The agency continues to produce actions (including evil ones — Ecc 8:12) that accumulate liability (Jer 2:35 — judgment) while the person remains unaware of the liability being created.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — The agency pattern reveals that refusal to repent is an *agency-investment process*: each agentive act performed within the sinful course (serving Baal, walking in Jeroboam's sins) represents an investment of agency in the wrong direction. The accumulation of these investments makes the sinful course progressively more entrenched — the person has more of themselves invested in it, making departure ever more demanding. This is why long-established refusal to repent (lifetime — 2Ki 15:18; generational — 2Ki 15:9) is harder to reverse than recent refusal: more of the person's agency-history is invested in the sinful course.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The agency pattern reveals that habitual defection is *agentively productive in the wrong direction*: the habitually sinning person builds, invests, and institutionalises — their agency is not diminished but its outputs are wrong. Hos 8:11 is the paradigm: full agentive investment in altars produces the self-reinforcing sinning structure. This reveals that breaking habitual defection requires redirecting a fully operative agentive capacity, not simply restoring an impaired one. The difficulty of breaking habitual defection is partly the difficulty of redirecting an established agentive investment pattern rather than initiating agency from scratch.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The agency pattern reveals that contagious sin produces *collective moral agency shaped toward sin*: the community is not merely influenced but is agentively sinning together, provoking God through their collective action (1Ki 14:22 — "provoked him to jealousy with their sins"). The communal scale of the agency (a whole nation sinning together through collective agentive action) makes contagious sin's moral consequence larger than any individual's sinning. Ecc 9:18 (CHAR-6 corpus adjacent) — "one sinner destroys much good" — is the wisdom-register observation about a related pattern: the contagious sin corpus demonstrates this through the specifics of national-scale moral consequence.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The agency pattern reveals that political revolt is fundamentally an *assertion of political agency*: the revolting community reclaims governing self-determination by breaking submission and establishing independent rule. The pa.sha act is the form through which political agency asserts itself against an established governing relationship. This explains why revolt is more than passive non-compliance — it is an active assertion of independent agency through the decisive act of breaking submission and constructing alternative governance.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — The agency pattern reveals that sinful speech is a fully agentive act for which the speaker is accountable: Mat 26:65 — "He has uttered" — the utterance is attributed to the speaker as a deliberate agentive act. This is why Mat 12:31's unforgivable blasphemy against the Spirit is foregrounded as such: it is a specific, deliberate agentive speech-act that carries permanent consequence. Sinful speech is not accidental or non-agentive; it is a chosen deployment of speech-agency in the wrong direction, for which the agent bears full accountability.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The agency pattern reveals that CHAR-10's mechanisms engage agency across a spectrum: enticement progressively captures agency through desire (the person becomes less free as the desire deepens — 2Pe 2:14 "insatiable for sin"); hypocrisy misdirects agency through social pressure (Gal 2:13 — led astray); apostasy exercises agency in departure (Act 21:21). The most severe form for agency is the trained-greed enticement: the person whose heart has been "trained in greed" has an agency that is now shaped by the training — their choices naturally move toward the sinful appetite because the agency-forming process has been captured.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The agency pattern reveals that the universal sin-condition does not render persons passive recipients but *active participants* in their own enslavement: "you present your members to sin as instruments" (Rom 6:13) — the person is doing the presenting. The condition's enslavement operates through the person's own agentive acts, which are the form the enslavement takes. This has a significant implication: the condition is not external imposition but internal captivity that operates through and as the person's own actions — making moral accountability real (they sin — Rom 3:23) even within the context of structural enslavement.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The agency pattern reveals that sin's enslaving power does not render the person passive but captures active agency and deploys it within the sin-domain. The practicing-sinner is agentively sinning (Joh 8:34); the enslaving power captures and redirects what remains an active agentive capacity. This is why the enslaving power is so difficult to escape from within: the person's agency is engaged, not dormant — but it operates within the master's domain rather than toward freedom. Liberation requires an external intervention (new birth — 1Jo 3:9; Christ's protection — 1Jo 5:18) that restructures the agency's constitutional direction.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The agency pattern reveals that sin as divine record is the product of genuine human agency: the record is filled by real agentive choices (drawing sin, adding sin, walking in sin, not departing). This is what makes the record a moral account rather than a fatalistic condition: each entry was made by the person's own agency. This agency-grounding of the record is also what makes genuine penitential agency possible: the turn of Eze 18:21 is meaningful precisely because the prior record was genuinely the person's own.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — The agency pattern reveals that the characteristic is not a passive reception of divine action but a genuine agentive co-participation: the person must act (repent, believe, bring the offering, lay hands) for the forgiveness-transaction to be enacted. The divine side is equally agentive (God forgives, Christ's blood is poured out), but the human agency is not bypassed. The characteristic is thus a cooperative agentive event: human agency in seeking + divine agency in granting = forgiveness sought and received.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The agency pattern reveals that generational sin is not passive reception of a condition but requires active volitional-agentive participation: each generation actively rebels (Psa 78:17 — "rebelling") and "sins still more" (Psa 78:32). The generational pattern is thus sustained by genuine human agency at each stage, which is why the response also requires genuine agency — the active heart-turning (1Ki 8:47) rather than passive waiting for the inherited condition to dissolve.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The agency pattern reveals that the sinner as moral character is an *active* rather than passive identity: sinners entice others (Pro 1:10), direct hostility against Christ (Heb 12:3), speak harsh things against God (Jude 15), and lend/do good/love within their radius (Luk 6:32/33/34). The sinner-identity is not a condition of passivity or mere moral weakness but a constituted inner orientation that actively expresses itself in agentive ways — both against God (Heb 12:3; Jude 15) and within the self-interest radius (Luk 6:32/33/34).

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The agency pattern reveals the characteristic's most productive inner dynamic: guilt-recognition produces a specific agentive sequence — bringing an offering → confession → restitution — that is the conscience-to-action pipeline. The guilt-state is the inner impetus that keeps the pipeline flowing; without guilt-recognition, the agentive repairs would not occur. This is the characteristic's designed function (T0.2.1): equipping the person to recognise moral failure and initiate repair.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — The agency pattern reveals CHAR-18's most distinctive feature: the transformation of the person from active agent of iniquity (Isa 5:18 — drawing iniquity) to passive subject of iniquity's power (Isa 64:6/7 — taken away, melted in its hand). The accumulated iniquity reverses the agency relationship: the person who chose the crime is eventually chosen by it — ensnared (Pro 5:22), swept away (Isa 64:6), and melted (Isa 64:7). Only external divine agency can reverse this (Mic 7:19 — God treads iniquities underfoot; Zec 3:9 — removes iniquity in a single day).

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — The agency pattern confirms the volitional-enslavement dynamic: the person who actively transgressed (agentive commission) is progressively incapacitated in their agency (Pro 29:6 — ensnared; Psa 19:13 — dominion threatened). The active agency at commission (Amos 4:4 — "come and transgress") is replaced by the passivity of being held in transgression's grip (Pro 29:6). This is the characteristic's most significant inner-being consequence: the agent of the crossing becomes the captive of what they crossed into.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — The agency pattern confirms CHAR-20's distinctive communal-reach: the agency of a single faithless person implicates and damages the agency of the whole community (Jos 7:1 — Achan; 1Ch 10:13 — Saul's faithlessness affecting all Israel). This communal-agency reach distinguishes CHAR-20 from characteristics whose consequences remain primarily individual. The leader's faithlessness (2Ch 28:22 — Ahaz; 2Ch 26:16 — Uzziah; 2Ch 36:14 — priests and people) consistently radiates to the community, showing that the agency of CHAR-20's faithlessness is constitutively social.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — The agency pattern reveals CHAR-21's most distinctive inner-being arc: the transition from active evil-devising agent (Pro 6:14; Isa 32:7) to passive instinct-driven captive (Jude 10 — destroyed by instinct; 2Pe 2:19 — enslaved). The reversal of agency — from the person who perverts to the person destroyed by their own perversion — is CHAR-21's defining developmental trajectory. The perverter becomes the perverted-by-their-own-perversion.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — The agency pattern reveals that CHAR-22 is M10's most agentively other-directed characteristic: every expression of injustice requires a victim and an agent. Job 29:17 — "I broke the fangs of the unrighteous" — the metaphor names the unjust as a predatory agent; Psa 58:2 — "your hands deal out violence on earth" — the active distribution of violence across the earth. CHAR-22's agent is not merely a person who does wrong but a person who systematically does wrong *toward* others — its defining agentive orientation is harm-toward-persons.

---

## T3.8 — Moral Evaluation

### T3.8.1

**Question:** Does this characteristic engage the moral evaluation faculty — the capacity to assess against a standard of right, wrong, good, and true — and if so, how?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Moral evaluation is definitionally engaged: the "wilful" qualifier requires that the person knows what they are doing is wrong — which presupposes a moral evaluation faculty that has assessed the act. Gen 39:9 — Joseph's moral evaluation names the act as "great wickedness" and "sin against God" — two moral evaluation registers (magnitude: great wickedness; relational: against God). Deu 19:15 — legal testimony as a formal moral evaluation mechanism. The cultic system (VCG-04) is a structured moral-evaluation apparatus: the community's obligation is triggered when the sin "becomes known" (Lev 4:14) — a community-level moral evaluation event.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Moral evaluation is engaged in the realisation phase, not during the sinning act. Lev 5:5 ("when he realizes his guilt") — "realizes his guilt" is a moral evaluation event: the person assesses the past act against the moral standard and concludes that it was a violation. Balaam (Num 22:34) — "I have sinned" — names the moral evaluation conclusion. In the sinning act itself, moral evaluation was not operative because the relevant information was absent.

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Moral evaluation is definitionally engaged: confession is the act of evaluating one's own past act as sin. 2Sa 24:10 — "I have sinned greatly in what I have done... I have done very foolishly" — two moral evaluative judgments: (1) the act was great sin; (2) it was foolish — both evaluative verdicts applied to his own conduct. Num 12:11 — "we have done foolishly and have sinned" — Aaron evaluates his act by moral character (foolishness) before naming it as sin. Dan 9:5/8/11 — the extended confession applies multiple evaluative categories ("done wrong," "acted wickedly," "rebelled," "shame belongs to us") — a rich moral evaluation deployed against the confessor's own history.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Moral evaluation is engaged through its *failure and distortion*. Jer 2:35 — "I am innocent" is a moral evaluation verdict: the person has assessed their own moral standing and concluded "innocent." The verdict is wrong, but the faculty is operating — it is producing a false output. Ecc 8:12 — the sinner who observes their own long life during repeated evil and implicitly concludes "things are fine" is performing a moral evaluation: outcome-as-verdict. Tit 3:11 — "self-condemned" — the person's conscience (moral evaluation faculty) has implicitly produced a correct verdict ("condemned") that the person does not consciously receive.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Moral evaluation is engaged in its distorted mode. 1Ki 16:31 — "as if it had been a light thing" — the moral evaluation faculty is producing a verdict about the gravity of the sin (light/trivial) that is false. The false verdict enables the refusal: if the sin is "light," there is little impetus to depart from it. Heb 10:26 — the person has received "the knowledge of the truth" — the correct moral evaluation is available, but the will acts against it. The moral evaluation faculty is engaged (it produces outputs) but either produces false verdicts (minimisation mode) or its correct verdicts are bypassed by the will (deliberate sinning mode).

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — The moral evaluation faculty is engaged in the distorted assessments habitual defection produces. 1Ki 8:46 — "there is no one who does not sin" — Solomon's prayer uses this universal moral assessment as the basis for requesting a divine provision: the moral evaluation is accurate (universal sinning is real) and is the ground of the petition for mercy. Ecc 9:18 — "one sinner destroys much good" — an external moral evaluation of the destructive capacity of the habitual sinner, assessing the sinner's influence on the surrounding moral environment. The habitual sinner's own moral evaluation is not directly evidenced in the corpus, but Pharaoh's resumed sinning (Exo 9:34) implies a moral evaluation that concluded the period of constraint (the plague) was no longer operative.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — The moral evaluation faculty is engaged through its *suppression* in the community and its *misuse* in the leader. For the leader: 1Ki 16:2 — "you have walked in the way of Jeroboam" — the leader has evaluated Jeroboam's sinning path as acceptable and followed it; the moral evaluation faculty produces a false verdict (this is acceptable leadership). For the community: the transmitted sinning becomes the normalised standard against which moral evaluation operates; 1Ki 14:22 — "more than all that their fathers had done" — the community escalates beyond the transmitted pattern, showing that the moral evaluation faculty is calibrated against the wrong baseline (the transmitted sinning course rather than God's law).

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(E)*

**[CHAR-8]** E — The moral evaluation faculty is engaged through the narrator's theological assessment. 2Ch 21:10 — "because he had forsaken the Lord, the God of his fathers" — the narrator's causal explanation applies a moral evaluation to the ruler's inner spiritual state (forsaking God) as the grounds for the political consequence (revolts occurring). The narrator's moral assessment frames the political revolt within a divine-moral framework: the governing authority's moral-spiritual failure has produced the political fragmentation.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Moral evaluation is engaged through its distortion in producing sinful speech. Mar 2:7 / Joh 10:33 — the scribes and Pharisees apply a correct moral-theological principle ("who can forgive sins but God alone?" / "you, being a man, make yourself God") to a false conclusion: their moral evaluation faculty reaches the wrong verdict (Jesus blasphemes) because they cannot accurately evaluate his identity. 1Ti 6:4 — "understands nothing" — the moral evaluation faculty is cognitively impaired, producing distorted moral verdicts that issue in slanderous speech.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — The moral evaluation faculty is engaged in each mechanism. (1) *Enticement*: Jam 1:14 — "each person is tempted when he is lured and enticed by his own desire" — the moral evaluation faculty is the implicit standard against which the enticement is measured; the person who is enticed has a moral evaluation faculty that could recognise the lure as a lure, but enticement bypasses this faculty by engaging desire first. (2) *Hypocrisy*: Gal 2:13 — "led astray by their hypocrisy" — Barnabas's moral evaluation faculty failed to correctly assess the situation; the collective hypocrisy distorted his moral evaluation through social pressure. (3) *Apostasy*: Act 21:21 — "forsake Moses" — the apostasy involves a moral evaluation that has concluded the covenantal commitment can be departed from.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The moral evaluation faculty is engaged through the condition's relationship to the law as a moral-evaluative standard. Rom 3:20 — "through the law comes knowledge of sin" — the law functions as the moral evaluation framework that reveals the condition; the person's moral evaluation faculty, informed by the law, recognises what sinning is. Rom 14:23 — "whatever does not proceed from faith is sin" — the moral evaluation faculty (calibrated by faith) determines whether an act participates in the condition or not. Joh 9:41 — "your guilt remains" — the moral evaluation of the Pharisees' own condition is the issue; they evaluate themselves as not guilty while the condition persists.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The moral evaluation faculty is engaged through its distortion in the enslaved condition. Joh 8:33 — the Pharisees' claim never to have been enslaved (the context of Joh 8:34) — their moral evaluation of their own condition is wrong; the enslaving power has distorted their moral self-assessment. 1Jo 3:8 — "of the devil" — the apostolic moral evaluation declares an identity that the enslaved person themselves does not recognise as their own; the enslaving power has captured the moral evaluation faculty from generating the correct self-verdict.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The moral evaluation faculty is engaged through the record's accusation function. Isa 59:12 — "our sins testify against us" — the sins function as witnesses in a moral-legal adjudication; the moral evaluation faculty is the target of this testimony. Jer 16:10 — the people's moral evaluation of their own conduct ("What is our sin?") fails to identify what the divine record contains; the moral evaluation faculty is impaired. 1Sa 12:19 — "we have added to all our sins this evil" — the people's moral evaluation correctly identifies the record as cumulative and dangerous; this is the positive function of the faculty.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Yes — the moral evaluation faculty is engaged on both sides. On the seeking side: Lev 4:14 — "when the sin which they have committed becomes known" — the moral evaluation must recognise the committed sin as sin before seeking can begin; Exo 32:31 — Moses morally evaluates the golden calf as "a great sin" before God. On the receiving side: Luk 7:47 — "he who is forgiven little, loves little" — implies a moral evaluation of the magnitude of forgiveness received; the person who evaluates themselves as needing little forgiveness receives and loves less.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — Yes — through the corporate conscience-recognition in confession. 1Ki 8:47 — "we have sinned and have acted perversely and wickedly" — the moral evaluation faculty correctly identifies the condition ("sinned... perversely... wickedly") in the context of corporate confession; Jer 8:14 — "because we have sinned against the Lord" — moral causation is correctly identified. Isa 64:5 — "shall we be saved?" — the moral evaluation faculty raises the salvific question based on its recognition of the prolonged sinning-condition.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — Yes — the moral evaluation faculty is engaged in determining who qualifies as a sinner. Joh 9:16/24/25 — the Pharisees evaluate and declare Jesus "a sinner"; the healed man refuses to render the verdict — the moral evaluation faculty is the faculty being exercised in both cases. Luk 13:2 — "do you think that these Galileans were worse sinners?" — Jesus addresses a moral evaluation error (the inference that suffering indicates greater sinfulness); Mar 2:17 — "those who are well have no need of a physician, but those who are sick" — Jesus reframes the moral evaluation of the sinner-category from moral-contamination to need-for-healing.

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — Yes — as the evaluative act that precedes guilt-recognition. Gen 42:21 — "in truth we are guilty" — the brothers' moral evaluation correctly assesses their past conduct as guilty; Gen 44:16 — "God has found out the guilt of your servants" — Judah's moral evaluation acknowledges divine discovery of guilt. Amo 8:14 — those "who swear by the Guilt of Samaria" — the community's moral evaluation has inverted: they treat the guilt-object (an idol) as worthy of oath-swearing.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Yes — through both the self-assessment and the divine assessment dimensions. (1) *Divine moral evaluation*: Gen 15:16 — God evaluates the Amorites' iniquity as "not yet complete" — a precise moral assessment; Ezr 9:13 — "God exacted less than our iniquities deserved" — a morally calibrated evaluation of deserved versus received punishment; (2) *Human moral evaluation*: Job 31:11/28 — "that would be an iniquity to be punished by the judges" — Job's moral evaluation categories iniquity as judicial crime; Jer 14:7 — "though our iniquities testify against us" — the prophetic moral evaluation that gives the iniquity a witnessing voice.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Yes — through the self-assessment and prophetic-declaration dimensions. (1) *Self-assessment*: Job 34:37 — "he adds rebellion to his sin; he claps his hands among us" — Elihu's moral evaluation of Job's transgression; Psa 51:3 — "I know my transgressions" — the honest self-moral-assessment; (2) *Denial*: Pro 28:24 — "that is no transgression" — the morally evaluating faculty deployed to deny; Jer 2:29 — "why do you contend with me?" — moral evaluation inverted to self-justification; (3) *Prophetic declaration*: Mic 3:8 — "to declare to Jacob his transgression and to Israel his sin" — the prophet's moral evaluation corrects the community's self-evaluation.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Yes — through the prophetic assessment and the communal verdict dimensions. Jer 3:11 — "faithless Israel has shown herself more righteous than treacherous Judah" — God's moral evaluation comparing two levels of faithlessness; Jer 3:20 — "as a treacherous wife leaves her husband, so have you been treacherous to me, O house of Israel" — the prophetic moral evaluation naming the faithlessness precisely; Psa 119:158 — "I look at the faithless with disgust, because they do not keep your commands" — the psalmist's moral evaluation produces disgust.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Yes — through the wisdom-hatred-of-perversion and the self-indictment dimensions. Pro 8:13 — "perverted speech I hate" — wisdom's moral evaluation explicitly names perversity as hated; Psa 38:6's confession and Dan 9:5 — "we have sinned and done wrong and acted wickedly" — the moral evaluation faculty engaged in corporate self-indictment; Pro 21:12 — "the Righteous One observes the house of the wicked; he throws the wicked down to ruin" — God's moral evaluation of perversity leading to judgment.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Yes — through the abomination judgments and the comparative moral-evaluation passages. Deu 25:16 — "all who act dishonestly are an abomination to the Lord your God" — God's moral evaluation as the standard; Pro 29:27 — "an unjust man is an abomination to the righteous" — the righteous person's moral evaluation generating disgust; 1Cor 6:9 — "the unrighteous will not inherit the kingdom of God" — the eschatological moral evaluation.

---

### T3.8.2

**Question:** Does this characteristic enable, deepen, bypass, or impair moral evaluation in the person?

**[CHAR-1] Wilful sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-1]** E — Wilful sinning *bypasses* moral evaluation in the act of commission. The will acts despite the available moral evaluation: Saul (1Sa 15:24) knew the command but obeyed the people's voice — the moral evaluation was available but overridden. Over time, sustained sinning *impairs* moral evaluation: Isa 1:4's people "despise the Holy One of Israel" — the moral evaluation faculty has become inverted, treating the holy as contemptible. This impairment trajectory from bypass (single act) to inversion (sustained pattern) is the moral-evaluation consequence of wilful sinning.

**[CHAR-2] Unintentional sinning** *(E)*

**[CHAR-2]** E — Unintentional sinning operates through the *bypassing* of moral evaluation at the act-moment — not because the faculty is impaired but because it lacks input. The moral evaluation faculty requires the morally relevant facts to function; without them, it cannot assess the act as prohibited. The realisation phase shows the faculty operating correctly once the information is available: it correctly identifies the act as sin and drives the confession response (Lev 5:5; Num 22:34).

**[CHAR-3] Confession** *(E)*

**[CHAR-3]** E — Genuine confession *deepens* moral evaluation: the act of naming sin precisely requires and exercises the moral evaluation faculty. The more specific and layered the confession, the more deeply moral evaluation has operated. Num 12:11 — "done foolishly" adds a characterological dimension to the evaluation; Mat 27:4 — "betrayed innocent blood" adds a moral-victim dimension. These are not mere formulas but precise moral evaluations applied to specific acts. Hollow confession (Deu 1:41) shows moral evaluation present but truncated — the evaluation does not extend to assessing the next proposed action ("we will go up and fight") against the same standard.

**[CHAR-4] Conscience suppression** *(E)*

**[CHAR-4]** E — Conscience suppression *impairs* moral evaluation specifically in its self-directed application. The moral evaluation faculty produces inaccurate verdicts when turned on the self: "I am innocent" (Jer 2:35 — false verdict), "things are fine because I am prospering" (Ecc 8:12 — false verdict). The faculty continues to operate but its outputs about the self are corrupted by the absence of accurate God-knowledge (1Cor 15:34) and the absence of the fear of God (Ecc 8:12) that would calibrate the evaluation correctly.

**[CHAR-5] Refusal to repent** *(E)*

**[CHAR-5]** E — Refusal to repent *distorts* moral evaluation through minimisation (1Ki 16:31) and *bypasses* it in the deliberate-sinning mode (Heb 10:26 — the evaluation is correct but the will overrides it). Sustained refusal may impair moral evaluation by habituating the person to the minimisation assessment until it becomes the default cognitive framework, making accurate evaluation progressively harder.

**[CHAR-6] Habitual defection** *(E)*

**[CHAR-6]** E — Habitual defection *distorts* moral evaluation within the person: the self-reinforcing structure of Hos 8:11 (altars for sinning producing more sinning) implies a moral evaluation that finds the altars acceptable or even desirable — the sinning course has been normalised such that the moral evaluation faculty no longer registers it as problematic. Exo 9:34's Pharaoh — who sinned yet again once the plague ceased — reveals a moral evaluation that assessed the absence of immediate pressure as permission to resume sinning; the moral evaluation is responding to external consequence rather than to moral truth.

**[CHAR-7] Contagious sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-7]** E — Contagious sin *distorts* the moral evaluation faculty by recalibrating the community's moral reference baseline from God's standard to the leader's transmitted sinning course. When the leader's authority-transmitted sinning becomes the operative norm, the community's moral evaluation assesses their own conduct against that norm: 1Ki 14:22 — they surpass even their fathers' sin, but their moral evaluation does not register this as extraordinary departure because the fathers' sin is already the operative baseline. The distortion accumulates generationally.

**[CHAR-8] Political revolt** *(S)*

**[CHAR-8]** S — The corpus does not evidence how political revolt affects the moral evaluation faculty of the revolting parties. The moral evaluation is supplied by the narrator (theological framing) rather than attributed to the inner moral assessment of those involved in the revolt.

**[CHAR-9] Sinful speech** *(E)*

**[CHAR-9]** E — Sinful speech *expresses and deepens* impaired moral evaluation. The distorted moral-evaluation outputs of the corrupted heart (false verdicts — Mat 15:19 "evil thoughts") find their completed form in slanderous or blasphemous speech. Once articulated, the false moral verdict consolidates in the speaker's self-understanding and is more difficult to retract. Mar 14:64 — "they all condemned him as deserving death" — the blasphemy-verdict once spoken becomes the ground of action; the moral evaluation is hardened by its verbal articulation.

**[CHAR-10] Specialised sinful mechanisms** *(E)*

**[CHAR-10]** E — (1) *Enticement bypasses moral evaluation*: Jam 1:14 — desire engages before moral evaluation deliberates; the mechanism exploits the pre-deliberative desire-faculty to capture the person before moral evaluation can assess the situation. (2) *Hypocrisy distorts moral evaluation*: Gal 2:13 — collective hypocrisy corrupts the moral evaluation of the socially conforming individuals; the social weight of the group's behaviour overrides individual moral assessment. (3) *Apostasy reflects impaired moral evaluation*: Act 21:21 — the forsaking of Moses implies a moral evaluation that has incorrectly assessed the covenantal obligation — the moral evaluation faculty has produced a false conclusion about the covenantal commitment.

**[CHAR-11] Sin as universal condition** *(E)*

**[CHAR-11]** E — The condition *distorts and bypasses* moral evaluation. Heb 3:13 — "hardened by the deceitfulness of sin" — the hardening of the heart that sin's deceitfulness produces is a moral-evaluative hardening: the person's capacity to correctly evaluate their own condition is progressively diminished. Joh 9:41 — the Pharisees' false evaluation of their own seeing (while the condition persists) shows the condition distorting self-moral-evaluation: they evaluate themselves as seeing when the condition has produced blindness. The condition's self-concealing nature (perceptive pattern — T3.1.3) is also a moral-evaluative concealment.

**[CHAR-12] Sin as enslaving power** *(E)*

**[CHAR-12]** E — The enslaving power *bypasses and distorts* moral evaluation: the enslaved person evaluates themselves as free (Joh 8:33 — "we have never been enslaved") while the enslaving power holds them. The moral evaluation faculty cannot accurately assess the enslaved condition from within it; the externally-declared moral assessment (Jesus in Joh 8:34; John in 1Jo 3:8) is required to supply what the enslaved person's own moral evaluation cannot generate.

**[CHAR-13] Sin as divine record** *(E)*

**[CHAR-13]** E — The record *reveals the failure of the moral evaluation faculty* most precisely: Job 13:23 — Job cannot evaluate the extent of his own record; Jer 16:10 — the people under judgment cannot evaluate their own sin; Hos 12:8 — prosperity impairs Ephraim's moral self-evaluation. The record exceeds the person's own moral evaluation capacity — God's assessment of the record is more accurate than the person's self-assessment (Amo 5:12 — "I know how many are your transgressions"). The characteristic thus reveals the inherent limitation of self-directed moral evaluation.

**[CHAR-14] Forgiveness sought and received** *(E)*

**[CHAR-14]** E — Forgiveness received *corrects and deepens* moral evaluation. The woman of Luk 7:47 whose sins were many and who loved much had a more accurate moral self-evaluation (she recognised the many sins) than the Pharisee who loved little (implicitly evaluating himself as needing little forgiveness). The received forgiveness deepens moral evaluation by confirming what the seeker's moral evaluation recognised: that the sin-record was real, was many, and required this specific act of forgiving grace.

**[CHAR-15] Generational sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-15]** E — The generational pattern *impairs* moral evaluation in the active sinning phase and *enables* it only under pressure of consequences. Psa 78:32 — "they still sinned; despite his wonders, they did not believe" — the moral evaluation faculty fails to correctly identify the significance of the wonders for the community's moral orientation. The moral evaluation faculty is only correctly engaged when the outer consequences are undeniable (1Ki 8:47 — in captivity; Jer 8:14 — facing doom). The generational pattern delays and distorts correct moral evaluation until catastrophe forces it.

**[CHAR-16] The sinner as moral character** *(E)*

**[CHAR-16]** E — The sinner-identity *distorts* moral evaluation in the observer. Joh 9:24 — the Pharisees' wrong verdict on Jesus; Luk 13:2 — the wrong inference about Galilean sinners and their suffering. The self-righteous person's moral evaluation of others as sinners is systematically unreliable (Jesus corrects it; Joh 9:25 — honest uncertainty is more truthful than false certainty). The sinner's self-evaluation, when conscience-engaged, is more accurate (Luk 5:8; Luk 18:13; 1Ti 1:15).

**[CHAR-17] Guilt as inner-being state** *(E)*

**[CHAR-17]** E — The guilt-state *clarifies moral evaluation* when conscience operates correctly and *distorts* it when suppressed. Gen 42:21 — the brothers' guilt-state sharpens their moral evaluation of past conduct; they now correctly assess what they did as guilty. Eze 9:9 — "they say, 'The Lord does not see'" — the community's suppressed guilt produces an inverted moral evaluation: they misassess God's awareness and therefore their accountability. The guilt-state's clarifying effect on moral evaluation is its productive mode; its distorting effect (when guilt is suppressed) is its destructive mode.

**[CHAR-18] Iniquity as accumulated moral crime** *(E)*

**[CHAR-18]** E — Iniquity *distorts* the self-directed moral evaluation faculty. Psa 36:2 — "he flatters himself in his own eyes that his iniquity cannot be found out and hated" — the wicked person's self-directed moral evaluation cannot accurately assess the iniquity. Hos 12:8 — "they cannot find in me iniquity" — the moral evaluation of self is systematically skewed by wealth and pride. Conversely, the other-directed moral evaluation (Jer 14:7 — "our iniquities testify against us") is more accurate: the prophetic tradition's evaluation of the community's iniquity is correct. Iniquity impairs self-evaluation; it does not impair accurate evaluation by conscience or prophetic witness.

**[CHAR-19] Transgression as deliberate boundary-crossing** *(E)*

**[CHAR-19]** E — Sustained transgression *inverts* the moral evaluation faculty. Pro 28:24 — the moral evaluation faculty is used to deny transgression rather than recognise it; Jer 2:29 — the evaluating faculty produces self-justification ("why do you contend with me?") rather than self-recognition. The sustained transgressor's moral evaluation is not eliminated but redirected: it becomes skilled at moral rationalisation in the self-direction. The remedy restores accurate self-evaluation: Psa 51:3 — "I know my transgressions" — the conscience-engaged penitent's accurate moral self-assessment.

**[CHAR-20] Faithlessness as covenant-breaking sin** *(E)*

**[CHAR-20]** E — Faithlessness *distorts* self-directed moral evaluation while leaving other-directed evaluation intact. 2Ch 28:22 — Ahaz becoming more faithless under distress cannot accurately evaluate his own condition; 2Ch 26:16/18 — Uzziah overstepping priestly bounds until the priests challenge him — the self-directed moral evaluation was impaired by pride. Conversely: Psa 119:158 — "I look at the faithless with disgust" — the faithful person's moral evaluation of others' faithlessness is acute; Jer 3:11 — God's comparative moral evaluation is precise.

**[CHAR-21] Perversion as inner inversion** *(E)*

**[CHAR-21]** E — Perversion *inverts* the moral evaluation faculty. Isa 29:16 — the creature evaluating the potter as if the clay could assess the maker — the most extreme moral-evaluation inversion: the creature's evaluative faculty is so perverted it assesses the creator incorrectly. Pro 19:3 — "his heart rages against the Lord" after his own folly ruins his way — the moral evaluation faculty wrongly assigns blame outward to God rather than inward to the self. Pro 2:14 — "delight in the perverseness of evil" — the evaluative faculty assesses perversity as delightful rather than shameful.

**[CHAR-22] Injustice as moral failure of right conduct** *(E)*

**[CHAR-22]** E — Injustice *inverts* the self-directed moral evaluation faculty while the God-centred moral evaluation remains intact in others. Zep 3:5 — "the unjust knows no shame" — self-directed moral evaluation eliminated; Pro 29:27 — "an unjust man is an abomination to the righteous" — the righteous person's moral evaluation of the unjust is acute.
